you can obtain as well or better than elsewhere on earth

SOME RECENT BOOKS.
Labour and Luxury, a reply to “ Merrie Eng-
land,” by Nemo (paper, Is 3d), takes as its
motto a pregnant sentence by Frederic Harri-
son : “ There is a safe and a dangerous Socialism
before us. And of all our public wants none is
so urgent as wise teaching about the limits and
the consequences of these two.” The book
itself is a readable counterblast to Blatchford’s
vague and foolish rhetoric. Its strength is
chiefly due to quotation and the thoughts sug-
gested by quotation ; but the writer marshals
authorities and arguments with some dexterity.
A Memoir of Gladstone, by H. W. Lucy (cloth,
Is 4d), is cheap and readable, and has a good
portrait of the clever old man. Lucy is gener-
ally lucid, and has the story-telling knack;
though his standpoint is conventional and his
comment feeble and common-place. Sentences
like the following (p. 239) show only that the
most practised hand may slip occasionally :
“ To the gifts of a mobile countenance, a voice
sonorous and flexible, and a fine presence, Mr.
Gladstone possesses dramatic instincts, ” &c.
The Adventures of Harry Richmond (paper,
lOd ; cloth, 4s), published in Bell’s Indian anc
Colonial Library, is only the first of a flight
cheap Merediths, which should help to make
familiar to the multitude the genius of the
greatest living writer of English Action.
Marion Crawford’s The Ralstons, H. S. Cunnin
ham’s Sibylla, and Thos. Hardy’s Far from ti e
Madding Crowd, come to hand in “Macmillan
Colonial Library ” (paper, 2s lOd)—that exce
lent and deservedly popular collection. The
first-named, as also “ Rolf Boldrewood’s
Sphinx of Eaglehawk (issued by the same pu )-
Ushers; canves, 2s 3d), has previously bee
noticed. Sibylla is a tale of slight interest anc
no value ; and The Madding Crowd a welcom
re-issue of one of Hardy's best romances—a fin
piece of work which takes semi-classical rank
Burning Questions, by W. Peart- Robinson (pape
Is 3d), is chiefly intended for the British public
In several clear and readable essays the autho: ■
advocates that Freetrade should give way to
Fairtrade, which is to pave the way for imperia
customs-union.
A Primer of Evolution, by Edward Clodd (cloth
Is 9d), is an abridgment of “The Story o !
Creation, ”by the same author—a useful disciple
and interpreter of Darwin, Haeckel, and Huxley
The little book is admirably done, within its
limits; and serves well as an introduction t
further study.
Boot and Saddle Stories, by W. Ferrars Aubur
(Ward, Lock and Bowden; paper, Is 3d), is
collection of sketches of life in the army whic i
•have conceivably some interest for armyreaders,
but little or none for the general workman" a;
literature.

Some Books Worth Reading.
In the Midst of Life, by Ambrose Bierce.
Boards, 2s 6d. Some wonderfully fine
short stories.
The Woman in White, by Wilkie Collins.
Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is 3d. The author’s
best book, well printed.
The Cloister and the Hearth, by Charles
Reade. Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is 3d.
Tess of the D’Urbervilles, by Thomas Hardy.
Paper, 2s lOd ; cloth, 4s, A masterpiece
of English fiction.
Crime and Punishment, by Fedor Dostoieff-
sky. Paper, 3s. One of the most re-
markable of Russian realistic novels.
Noughts and Crosses, by A. T. Quiller-
Couch ; cloth, 5s 6d. Beautiful sketches
and stories.
The Malay Archipelago, by A. R. Wallace,
Paper, 2s lOd ; cloth, 3s lOd. A delight-
ful book of travel.
The Silver Domino ; cloth, 4s. A book of
amusing impertinences regarding liteiary
gods and prophets, now in its 22nd edition.
Lorna Doone, by R. D. Blackmore. Cloth,
3s. A charming English romance.
Looking Backward, by Edward Bellamy.
Paper, 9d. The bane or the antidote.
Pictures of the Socialistic Future, by Eugene
Richter. Paper, Is 3d. The antidote or
the bane.
The Green Carnation. Paper, 45,; cloth,
6s. 6d. Witty and amusing.
Studies in Pessimism, by Schopenhauer.
Paper, Is. 3d. ; cloth (larger selection), 2s.
10d. Delightfully cynical.
Yirginibus Puerisque, by R. L. Stevenson,
Cloth, 6s. 6d. Essays perennially charm-
ing.
My Lady Nicotine, by J. M. Barrie. Cloth,
6s. 6d, A book chief among ten thousand
and altogether lovely.
In a Canadian Canoe, by Barry Pain. Cloth,
4s. Sketches, verses and stories varying
in merit, but with keen thought and a
clever style at the back of all.
Prose Fancies, by Richard Le Gallienne.
Cloth, 6s. 6d. A recent book of idyllic
essays.
Post-Prandial Philosophy, by Grant Allen,
Cloth, 4s. Readable essays.
The Autocrat of the Breakfast Table. The
Professor at the Breakfast Table. The
Poet at the Breakfast Table. By Dr.
O. W. Holmes. Paper, 9d each ; cloth,
Is 3d, and (better edition) Is 9d.
The Manxman, by Hall Caine (cloth, 45.).
A really fine novel, epic in breadth and
intensity. Also, The Bondman, 4s. ; The
Scapegoat, 4s.
The Tales of John Oliver Hobbes (including
“Some Emotions and a Moral,” “A Study
in Temptations,” “A Bundle of Life,”
and “The Sinner’s Comedy”). Piquant
and clever. A charming edition, with
portrait of author ; cloth/6s. 6d.
A Child of the Age, by Francis Adams.
Cloth, 4s 4d.
Kenelm Chillingly, by Bulwer Lytton. His
best novel; cloth, 4s Id (recommended) :
also, cloth, 2s 6d.
Ships that Pass in the Night, by Beatrice
Harraden. Paper, 2s lOd; cloth, 4s.
Novel and clever.
Paradoxes, by Max Nordau. Paper, 3s.
Acute, fresh, thoughtful essays on many
interesting subjects.
National Railways, by James Hole. Cloth,
5s 6d. A full, instructive and interesting
discussion of the State railway question.
Papa, Mamma, and Baby, by Gustave Droz
(English translation). A witty, naughty,
delightful book, which should be first upon
every list of wedding presents. Cloth,
2s 9d.
Short Stories, by Guy de Maupassant. Paper,
Is 3d. Witty and wicked.
Labour and the Popular Welfare, by W. H.
Mallock. A clever and instructive essay
from the anti-socialist standpoint. Paper,
Is 4d.
Isis Very Much Unveiled, by Edmund Gar-
rett. A scathing exposure of the Mahatma
forgeries ; paper, Is 3d.
The Yellow Book, the new unconventional
magazine, established for the express pur-
pose of giving J)lrs. Grundy fits. Yols. 1,
2,3, 4 ; cloth, 6s 6d each.

memoir. Paper, Is 3d; cloth, 3s 9d.
Vivid, intense, thrilling ; a book to be
prized by every democrat

Red Letter Stories.
The Odd Number. Thirteen stories by Guy
de Maupassant, a choice selection (chiefly
from the “Contes de Lundi ”), and ex-
hibiting a side of the author’s genius not
shown in the “Short Stories ” elsewhere
listed.
A Saint, and other tales, by Paul Bourget,
The “other tales” are “A Gambler,”
“Two Little Boys,” and “ Childhood
Perfidy”—all characteristic of the author’s
limpid method.
The Crime of Sylvestre Bonnard, by Anatole
France.
Dame Care, by Hermann Sudermann.
The House by the Medlar-Tree, by Giovanni
Verga.
Gallegher, and other stories, by Richard
Harding Davis.
Balaam and his Master, and other sketches
and stories, by doel Chandler Harris,
*** With the doubtful exception of “The
Odd Number,” the stories in this series
are rather recommended by style and
sympathy than by plot o? vivid interest.
Davis and Harris have considerable Ameri-
can vogue, justified by their drollery and
perception ; but the average reader finds
them tending to dullness. Yerga, Suder-
mann, and Anatole France employ the
charm of| simplicity, not the fascination of
strength and splendour. Bourget’s stories
are lilies, not roses of literature—unless it
be white moss-roses. De Maupassant’s
striking book can be recommended for
general purchase ; the others require some
“ acquired taste,” some welcome of appre-
ciative culture. Paper, 3s. each.

We wish it to be understood that the character of the
business we do, and the absolutely Inclusive variety ol the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, considering the low prices we
ask lor everything we sell, and the consequent blessings
we are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves
PUBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life—honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
(or the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in hold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the tew
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
lit the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
neveroease from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
somewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they'
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
HAYMARKET (ONLY).
Patent Bicycles.

'The Bulletin’s Scrap-Book .
AN UNTIMELY THOUGHT.
I wonder what day of the week —
I wonder what month of the year—
Will it be midnight, or morning,
. And who will bend over my bier ?
What a hideous fancy to come
As I wait at the foot of the stair,
While Lilian gi ves the last touch
To her robe, or the rose in her hair!
Do I like your new dress—Pompadour ?
And do I like you ? On my life,
You are eighteen, and not a day more,
And have not been six years my wife.
Those two rosy boys in the crib
Upstairs are not ours, to be sure—
You are just a sweet bride in her bloom,
All sunshine, and snowy and pure.
As the carriage rolls down the dark street
The little wife laughs and makes cheer —
But —l wonder what day of the-week.
I wonder what month of the year I
T. B. ALDRICH.
PALABRAS CARINOSAS.
Good-night! I have to say good-night
To such a host of peerless things !
Good-night unto the fragile hand
All queenly with its weight of rings;
Good-night to fond, uplifted eyes,
Good-night to chestnut braids of hair,
Good-night unto the perfect mouth
And all the sweetness nestled there —
The snowy hand detains me ! then
I’ll have to say good-night again.
But there will come a time, my love,
When, if I read our stars aright,
I shall not linger by this porch
\\ ith my adieus. Till then, good-night!
You wish the time were now ? And I.
You do not blush to wish it so ?
You would have blushed yourself to death
To own so much a year ago—
What, both these snowy hands ! ah, then,
. I’ll have to say good-night again.
T.B. ALDRICH.
[Thomas Bailey Aldrich, born 1836, is one of the most
accomplished ef American litterateurs. In ’Bl be suc-
ceeded W. D. Howells as editor of the Atlantic Monthly.]
THREE SONNETS.
What is a sonnet ? ’Tis the pearly shell
That murmurs of the far-off murmuring sea ;
A precious jewel carved most curiously ;
It is a little pictu-re painted well.
What is a sonnet? ’Tis the tear that fell
From a great poet’s hidden ecstasy ;
A two-edged sword, a star, a song—ah me 1
Sometimes a heavy-tolling funeral bell.
Tliis was the flame that shook with Dante’s breath,
The solemn organ whereon Milton played,
And the clear glass where Shakespeare’s
shadow falls ;
A sea this is—beware who ventureth !
For like a fjord the narrow floor is laid
Mid ocean deep to the sheer mountain walls.
1844.
RICHARD WATSON GILDER.
The world is too much with ns ; late and soon,
Getting and spending, we lay waste our powers:
Little we see in Nature that is ours ;
We have given our hearts away—a sordid boon.
This sea that bares her.bosom to the moon ;
' The winds that will be howling at all hours,
And are up-gathered now like sleeping flowers :
For this, for everything, we are out of tune;
It moves us not. Great God 1 I’d rather be
A Pagan suckled in a creed outworn;
So might I, standing on this pleasant lea,
Have glimpses that would make me less forlorn ;
Have sight of Proteus coming from the sea,
Or hear old Triton blow his wreathed horn.
1770—1850.
WILLIAM WORDSWORTH.
Mysterious Night ! when our first parenfknew
Thee from report divine, and heard thy name,
Did he not tremble for this lovely frame,
This glorious canopy of light and blue?
Yet, ’neath a curtain of translucent dew,
Bathed in the rays of the great setting frame*
Hesperus with the host of heaven came :
And lo ! Creation widened in man’s view !
Who could have thought such darkness lay con-
cealed
Within thy beams, O Sun ? or who could find,
Whilst fly and leaf and insect stood revealed,
That to such countless orbs thou mad’st us blind?
Why do we, then, shun Death with anxious strife?
If Light can thus deceive, wherefore not Life ?
1775-1841.
BLANCO WHITE.
“I wonder if, when one married, one
would like another woman to supply the baby
element.” From “Gallia,” a new novel, by
Muriel Dowie (who married not long ago and
supplied her own baby element).
Princess Louise, the only lady of the
British Royal Family who has graciously refrained
from increasing the population, was 47 last month.
According to law, however, she has eight more
years in which to make up for lost time.
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
“ The Duchess of York,” says an emotiona
and intensely loyal London paper, “is very super'
stitious about opals, and well she might be-the
engagement ring given her by the late Duke of
Clarence was set with these stones.” How de-
lighted the Duchess’s present Duke must be to
come across a little thing like that!
For rheumatism use Colemane and Sons
Eucalypte Special Oil.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere .
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Maoleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Blade and White.
Anyone supplying to Tub Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will he
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal ; —The Standard and Diggers
Lews Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also :—R. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.
Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, Queensland,
S. Australia, W. Australia and Ma or Hand.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Governments on Interprovincial News-
papers, the annual subscription outside N.S. Wales is
now £l, with 3s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can he sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY, MAY 25, 1895.
The Bi-metallic Craze.
The advent in Australia of Moreton
Frewen, the eloquent advocate of bimetal-
lism, has aroused a considerable amount of
interest in the complex and dreary subject
of the “meejum of exchange.” A good many
newspapers have been temporarily or per-
manently converted to the new creed—
which is also a very old creed indeed ; and
many miscellaneous cranks have begun to
worship Frewen’s god, and have resolved
that bi-metallism is the cure for all the
financial ills of mankind. The world is out
of joint, and has been so as far back as any-
body has ever heard of, and it is full of
cranks and faddists who believe that their
one single nostrum can cure everything and
everybody. The church prescribes dogma
as the one infallible remedy for poverty,
want of employment, unequal distribution of
wealth, dirt, disease, doubt, discontent,
ignorance, bad trade, bad laws, and every
other trouble. The teetotaller proposes to
cure all that has gone wrong by prohibition
and cold tea. The Foreign-trader of the
M‘Mxllan variety regards imported shoddy
as the universal panacea ; the Protec-
tionist of the old school has an equally
universal remedy in a protective tariff;
the Single-tax fanatic proposes to straighten
up everything under the sun by putting all
taxation on the soil ; and now a new and
enthusiastic howler has arisen in the land
who believes that all troubles have their
origin in the “meejum of exchange,” and
that when silver is made legal tender and its
value defined in a certain fixed ratio to that
of gold, this weary, soulless, sin-stained old
world will begin to go straight at last.
• • •
The average enthusiast only holds one
craze at a time, and his faith in that is a
profound and solemn thing. The aggra-
vated type of Single-taxer firmly believes
that there is healing and regeneration in his
nostrum alone, despite the fact that China
has been run on what is practically a Single-
tax basis for centuries, and hasn’t risen one
inch out of her old-time petrifaction. The
Freetrader pure and simple is confronted
by the fact that a dozen great nations winch
have tried his remedy have given it up in
despair, and of the few which still adhere to it
several have sunk into something approach-
ing beggary. The Tory Protectionist isn’t
troubled in the least by the fact that, even
in the countries which have grown rich in
the aggregate under his system, the struggle
for existence is about as desperate as it is
anywhere else, and that often the struggle
is hardest in the country where most riches
are accumulated. The churches keep on
prescribing faith as a medicine for every ill,
just as if the world had not had faith, more
or less, for 1900 miserable years; and the Cold
Tea fiend keeps on prescribing Prohibition,
regardless of the fact that the world has been
experimenting with Prohibition, and giving it
up in disgust for ages, and that almost every
land which has been discovered where
alcohol was unknown has been a land of
ignorance and barbarism and gore and
general blight, and that the Prohibitionist
himself is a gaunt, ill-omened warning to
other men not to follow in his footsteps.
And it matters nothing at all to the bi-
metallist that this globe has tried over and
over again in different places, mono-metal-
lism, bi-metallism, tri-metallism, other kinds
of metallism, hard money, soft money,
barter, and various other things, and has
been, on the whole, very much the same
kind of a globe through it all.
* • *
.Bi-metallism, on the whole, is not a sub-
ject which is calculated to excite much
popular enthusiasm. It is about the most
desolate-looking of all the single fads by
which man is always on the point of being
regenerated, and in spite of which he never
actually regenerates to any great extent.
The theory of Moreton Frewen and his
party is that it is possible, by an agreement
among all the leading nations of the world,
to fix gold and silver on a permanent rela-
tive value, which relative value they will
maintain without any fluctuations whatever.
If both metals are made legal tender on the
assumption that, say, 15|oz. of silver equal
loz. of gold, then it is claimed of
silver will be worth loz. of gold, and
will always continue so while the agreement
lasts. Even if the world should begin
to produce 30oz. of silver for loz. of gold,
reckoning only the quantities available for
coinage after all demands for other purposes
are satisfied, the price of silver is not to go
down ; and even if the production of the
white metal becomes only seven times, in-
stead of 15i times, that of gold, its price is
not to go up. The natural desire for the
smaller and more portable variety of coinage
is not to create any preference which will
upset the equilibrium. All this looks about
as promising as the theory that an inter-
national agreement could enact that 11b. of
tea shall always be equal in value to 15|lb.
of rock-salt, no matter what circumstances
may arise, and it also looks just about as
useful. Outside of a comparatively small
knot of enthusiasts, there are few people
who can see any more cause for rejoicing in
the fact that loz. of gold is worth 15|oz. of
silver, than in the fact that it is worth 15|
tons of road-metal or any other substance.
* • *
It is an acknowledged fact that since silver
ceased to be legal tender among the leading
commercial nations of Europe, the prices of
most articles of merchandise have seriously
decreased. This has been a heavy loss to
the producer and a considerable gain to the
consumer, and as the producer and the con-
sumer are, in the aggregate, the same indi-
vidual, things in this respect are practically
equal. Also —which is much more serious—
the change has been, in a sense, greatly to
the disadvantage of the nations which
borrow money, and great'y to the advantage
of those which lend it. If Australia were
to pay off her public debt, she would
require to export nearly twice as much pro-
duce to raise the required amount as she
would have had to do a few decades ago.
This, however, is much less of a disadvantage
than it looks. The payment of the public
debt is an event which shows no signs of
taking place for severalcenturies, and what the
state of things may be when the time arrives
—if it ever does arrive—is not a matter for
present consideration. It doubtless ought to
be a matter for present consideration, but it is
necessary to deal, not with the things which
should be, but with those that are. The
burden at the present time is not the prin-
cipal but the interest, and the rate of interest
has diminished so largely along with the
diminution of prices that the Australian
public debt of £200,000,000 odd is not much
more of a burden than a similar debt would
have been at the prices and the interest rates
of 40 years ago.
* * *
Bi-metallists claim that the afore-men-
tioned fall in prices is the direct consequence
of the demonetisation of silver, and that it
silver were again made legal tender they
would go up to their old standard, or some-
thing approaching thereto. So far as con-
cerns the producer and the consumer this
would matter little ; an all-round rise of,
say, 100 per cent., would leave these two
individuals practically just where they are
now. And if prices have fallen owing to the
demonetisation of silver, which is exceed-
ingly doubtful, there is just as good reason
to believe that interest has gone down from
the same cause, and would rise again in the
same fashion if the white metal were rehabi-
litated, in which case—so long as no
attempt is made to repay the prin-
cipal of the public debt—everybody
would be just about where he was before.
Also, bi-metalhsm takes no account of the
fact that, taking the world as a whole, silver
has not been demonetised—instead there is
more silver coin in circulation than there
ever was before. It ignores the fact
that prices were steadily declining long
before the date of the alleged demonetisation,
and that the decline was hardly accelerated
in the least by that more or less apocryphal
event. And, in assuming that the abolition
of silver as legal tender money in certairj
countries was the sole cause of the fall in
values, it assumes that nothing whatever
was owing to the great extension of
road, railway and steamship communication
which enables all manner of things to be
brought to market at greatly diminished
cost, the great increase of supplies
because improved mechanism is con-
stantly enabling each human unit to produce
more to-day than he did yesterday, and the
further great increase of production through
the opening-up of new lands in Australia,
Africa, South America, and other formerly
waste regions. The doctrine which assumes
that all these influences had nothing, or very
little, to do with the general reduction of
prices, and that the alleged demonetisation
of silver was the main cause of it all, makes
a very steep assumption indeed.
• * •
The reason for the bi-metallic gospel, put
into the fewest words, is that the practical
withdrawal of silver from coinage in certain
countries has so reduced the amount of avail-
able money that an artificial scarcity has
been created, and the value of gold has there-
fore been forced up to such an extent that
in most lines it now takes nearly twice as
much goods to buy a sovereign as it did a
few decades ago. And just here the silver
gospel falls heavily over the unaccountable
facts that there is now about three times as
much money in existence as there was before
the alleged demonetisation process started ;
that even in some of the countries where
silver was demonetised the amount of silver
coin has not, apparently, diminished; and
that, instead of a scarcity, the complaint is of
a superabundance of money lying idle in
banks at all the great centres of commerce,
and vainly seeking for a profitable outlet.
And, so far as concerns the frequent allega-
tion about the fall in the market value'of
silver since the frequently-aforementioned
demonetisation, it is a lamentable fact that
between 1600 and 1700, and again between
1700 and 1800, the fall in silver values was
almost as great as that during the present
century. These things may not prove that
the bi-metallic theory is wrong, but they
lend so much confusion to a subject that was
already about the most complex on record
that The Bulletin hereby suspends its
opinion indefinitely, and waits, though in an
unfavorable frame of mind, for further evi-
dence.
• # »
For the present, in fact, this paper regret-
fully declines to regard bi-metallism seriously.
Still it is open to conviction in the matter.
If the bi-metallists can prove that the de-
monetisation of silver was the cause of the
general decline in values, and not increased
production, steam, electricity, improved
machinery, cheaper and faster means of com-
munication, and the opening up of new
countries ; or even if they can prove that a
certain definite proportion of the decline was
owing to these causes, and that the silver
disaster was responsible for the rest, it will
probably view the matter in a wholly new
light. It will do the same if the alleged
scarcity of money, owing to the withdrawal
of silver, can be supported by any reasonable
evidence ; or if it can be proved that gold
and silver can be kept at any fixed ratio to
each other by any amount of international
agreement; or if anything else of value can
be proved relative to the case. Meanwhile
it recognises, in a purely selfish sense, that
bi-metallism would probably force up the
price of silver considerably, and at least for
a time, and that Australasia, as a silver-pro-
ducing country, would profit largely thereby,
while its big silver-deposits held out, and
assuming it did not lose more than it gained
through a corresponding depreciation of gold,
and providing nothing of an unforeseen charac-
ter ensued to spoil an otherwise beautiful cal-
culation. To that limited extent, and with a
solemn conviction that something of an un-
foreseen character probably would ensue,
it is a bi-raetallist—but no further. Bi-
metallism, regarded as a cure for things in
general, looks just as promising as the great
Cold-Tea theory, which is based on equally
convincing statistics —and as many other
theories. Even if it should come to
pass, which seems eminently improbable, it
will certainly produce very few of the results
expected ftom it. The precept which every
faddist should gum in his hat for constant
reference is that the world can’t be re-
generated by one idea, whether it be religious
dogma, prohibition, single-tax, freetrade,
protection, eight-hours, one-raan-one-vote,
bi-metallism, or anything else. Neither can
it be regenerated by ten ideas, or even by
several more. There are a good many hun-
dred things which want to be done before it
will be much the better, and the person who
preaches his one particular little shred of
gospel as a panacea is only an oppressive
bore and a disappointment. If bi-metallism
proves its case, then it can claim to rank as
one small item in a long programme of re-
form, but, so far, it hasn’t definitely proved
anything.
Tariff Questions.
You can’t make an omelette without breaking
eggs ; and, if Australia wants Federation, she
will have to pay the price. Federatioa will
certainly not be consummated without indi-
vidual hardship; and, though the community
as a whole will gain immensely, there is no
doubt that, in isolated cases, the loss will over-
shadow the profit. It is very likely, for
example, that Federation—with its inevitable
accompaniment of interprovincial Freetrade
means the destruction of the N.S.W. cane-
sugar industry. That industry is carried on in
the northern coastal districts un ler conditions
which prima-facie make competition with
Queensland sugar-growers possible only so lona
as it is protected. The low-lying lands of the
Clarence and Richmond, where the cane is
chiefly grown, are subject to destructive floods ;
cane-disease is prevalent; and the comparatively
cold climate reduces the sugar-contents of the
cane much below the average yielded in
Queensland. ,
* • *
Now that Treasurer Reid proposes to begin,
at the end of two years, to remove by slow in-
stalments the import duty of £3 per ton which
enables New South Wales to successfully com-
pete with Queensland and foreign-grown, the
manager of the Colonial Sugar Refining Co.
rises to ask the sugar-farmers “if they are
prepared to accept quietly the fate designed for
them,” and emphasises his question with a
threat of withdrawal of the co.’s capital from
their industry. Now, the Colonial Sugar Re-
fining Co. has a very large capital invested in
the New South Wales sugar-industry. It is by
far the largest sugar-miller and refiner in the
province, and probably the majority of the
farmers are dependent upon it either for land,
or for advances, or for crushing power. It is
also the largest sugar-miller and refiner in
Queensland and Fiji; it has refineries in Mel-
bourne, Adelaide and Auckland ; and it
acknowledges total assets of 207,369. It is,
in fact, one of the most successful results of
“private enterprise ” in Australasia ; and The
Bulletin allows it every credit for the men it
employs and the men it supports, and the
manner in which its business is conducted.
* • #
But, if there is one thing more clear than
another, it is that the sole object of the Col.
Sugar Refining Co. is, as might be expected, to
earn dividends for its shareholders. It does
not care two straws for anything but this ; and
its operations in every province move by dif-
ferent ways to the same end. It is, of course,
by far the largest employer of black labor in
Australasia ; but the manager’s ad captandum
appeal to New South Wales farmers does not
scruple to lay stress upon the great harm that
will be done to “white industry ” if the New
South Wales duty is removed. No doubt, if
the co. had its way, there would be complete
Australasian freetrade in (cane) sugar, and it
would then supply us— The Bulletin here
assumes that Fiji produces sugar much more
cheaply than New South Wales—almost exclu-
sively from its Fiji mills and plantations,
worked by black - labor. As every Aus-
tralasian province imposes a duty on sugar,
the co. has suited itself to circumstances, and
established mills, or refineries, or plantations
in every province.
* # •
The co. merely uses in New South Wales
now the threat which it found so effective in
Queensland in 1892. When Sam Griffith was
bending under the pressure of the banks to
extend the Polynesian Act, and there seemed
still a chance that he would declare in favor of
a white Australia, the Col. Sugar Co. threw
its weight into the scale with the emphatic
declaration that if black-labor were abolished,
it would be necessary to take its mills and
machinery, and all its Q. capital—upwards o #
a million —over to Fiji. That settled
indecision ; and the colored laborer has become
more and more of a curse in Queensland every
year since. The co. has a black face for Queens-
land ; but a white face for New South Wales.
* m *
The co.’s manager, in order to bolster up his
New South Wales case, says that there “all
the work ” connected with the sugar industry
“is done by white men”—which is not true.
T. Wilmett, of Townsville, accompanied a
Q. Ministerial party round the Tweed River
(N.S.W.) district last year, and informed the
Townsville Bulletin that “ the Kanakas who
have completed their time in Queensland, and
have settled on the Tweed, number about 400.
Many of these lease the land and cultivate it
on their own account, and are large employers
of white labor, to whom they pay the current
rate of wages —.£1 a week with board.” A cor-
respondent wrote The Bulletin of May 19,
’94, that “in the Richmond River sugar-cane
biz. the Caucasian will soon be starved out by
the Hindoo. About a hundred nigs. ‘ our
fellow-subjects,’ have leased farms here, and it
is reported that 300 more are on the way. . . .
For breakfast each man has a cupful of rice;
for dinner, a scone without butter, jam, beef,
or tea ; for supper, a stalk of sugar-cane.”
# # •
These considerations throw some doubt on
the unselfish zeal of the Colonial Sugar Co. for
white industry. The sugar-duties, in fact,
could have found no more unsatisfactory cham-
pion. Their defence must rest, not on the fact
that removal means ruin to the industry, for
Federation must come at whatever cost to indi-
viduals, but on the fact that removal would
merely throw the New South Wales trade into
the arms of Queensland, and it is to the interest
of Australia to fight the black-labor province as
a foreign foe, until it is forced to capitulate.
And advantage should be taken of the mainten-
ance of the duties to prohibit coloured labor in
connection with the sugar-industry in New
South Wales. That industry is speckled now •,
for all the Colonial Sugar Co. cares it will soon
be as piebald as its neighbour.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Rights of a Minority.
The interesting Q. Supreme Court case, Browne
v. Cowley, in which a member of Q. Leg.
Assembly sued the Speaker for damages, conse-
quent on his ejection on Sept. 12 last, resulted
in plaintiff being non-suited by Chief Justice
Griffith. The treatment Browne complained
of was simply the Boodlewraith majority’s
brutal and tyrannical use of its power. The
occasion was the discussion on the Coercion
Bill, under which martial law was proclaimed
in W. Q. Boodlewraith, hopeless of passing
the Bill in any other way, gagged, choked, and
throttled debate. The Speaker, the Chairman
of Committees, and the Acting-Chairman—all
red-hot supporters of the Government party —
crushed out opposition; and the mental condition
in which some of them were when “ presiding”
provoked even Brisbane Courier —a solid sup-
porter of the Government —to say that they
should not be permitted to preside again.
Chief Justice Griffith, in hearing the case,
first of all excluded all evidence of what took
place prior to Browne’s expulsion ; thus saving
the Government from an exposure of the dis-
creditable proceedings of its Acting-Chairman
of Committees. After much ingenious argu-
ment, the Judge held that, as the Speaker was
merely carrying out the will of the majority of
the House, based on a standing order confirmed
by the majority of the House, no action could
lie against him in the Supreme Court. If there
was any error of procedure, then the House
itself was the judge of procedure, without
appeal. Should this decision be sustained, it
simply means that a minority of members of Q.
Legislative Assembly have no Parliamentary
rights whatever as against a majority, which
may silence them, or exclude them from the de-
liberations of the House for as long as it chooses,
on the ground of contumacy. Contumacy may
mean any kind of opposition to the wishes of
the majority ; the majority are sole judges of
what is contumacy; they execute their own
sentence by passing a majority resolution with-
out debate, and without hearing the accused in
his defence ; and there is no appeal from their
decision. If this is constitutional law in
Queensland The Bulletin pities Queensland.
The whole case, by the w r ay, affords another
illustration of the rottenness of the system
which permits crooked-minded and unjudicial
Australian politicians to appoint themselves to
the Bench—as Griffith practically appointed
himself. When Griffith was Premier he was
identified with the gang which abolished,
in one act, Browne’s rights as a member
of Parliament ; he was warmly opposed by
Browne’s party ; and the Attorney-General
(who conducted the prosecution) was his col-
league and nominee. The Bulletin does not
believe that Chief Justice Griffith was con-
sciously influenced by any of these things ; but
no man can entirely divest himself of engrained
prejudices. The nett result, however, is that
Chief Justice Griffith is exceedingly unfit to
preside over such cases as that of Browne v.
Cowley ; since, however he might decide, the
public faith in the purity of the justice which
he dispenses is lessened or destroyed by the
knowledge of his political history and predi-
lections. ,
The Crushed Victorian Selector.
A Victorian writes to The Bulletin as fol-
lows :—The Vic. Lands Minister is now trying
to collect the rents owing to the State, and, as
might be expected from a too tardy action,
there is some uproar. In good times, selectors
were allowed to evade their liabilities to the
State; but, now that the Treasurer has a
deficit, the arrears are being called in. It was
a criminal mistake to allow the arrears to accu-
mulate, and it is equally a mistaken policy to
expect four or five years’ rent from selectors at
the close of a disastrous season. Irvine,
M.L.A., has his scheme to meet the difficulty.
Callinan, Surveyor-General, has his scheme ;
and the Argus also has a scheme. is strange
that these people cannot or will not see the
great, if not the sole, difficulty farmers are
laboring under. Every farmer can see it; but,
of course, farmers’ opinions do not count.
High-priced money is the sore point. The dear-
ness of money has been gradually swamping the
farmersuntiltheir heads are hardly above water.
As a result some of the most energetic of the
Vic. farmers are determined upon giving up the
struggle and leaving the land to the mortgagees.
I say, advisedly, the most energetic ; for, in
many districts, these people are in the greatest
difficulties. When districts were first broken
up, some of the selectors were idlers, while
others were energetic. The former borrowed
all they could on their land, and many had to
sell in theboom-time. Their moreenergetic neigh-
bors who had clear selections became purchasers,
and mortgaged their own land as well as the
selections they bought in order to raise the pur-
chase-money. Then came evil days : poor re-
turns, low prices, high interest; and the result
is that the flower of Victorian settlers are being
slowly ruined, chiefly by the heavy interest.
The cautious plodders, who were content with
what they had, are fairly able to meet their
liabilities. Mr. Irvine would make all this
right by deducting sixpence an acre from rents.
Such a concession is worthless, and only shows
the pettiness of the proposer. Callinan’s
plan is equally useless as a present remedy, and
the Argus remedies are Argus remedies. Even
purblind statesmen should see the remedy ; for if
any question was ever fully discussed in Victoria,
that of obtaining “ cheap money for farmers ”
on Credit Foncier lines was. Patterson saw
that it was popular ; but knavery, imbecility,
or both, prevented his Government from giving
it a fair chance; and so a scheme for the recon-
struction of the Savings Banks was brought
forward instead. The result was a fiasco. The
Turner Government preferred to get a commis-
sion to inquire into the workings of a State Bank,
&c. After resting for months, the Commiss >u
begins to examine experts who admit that Limy
do not know what a Slate Bank is. Yet six
weeks at most would bring a copy of Balfour’s
Irish Laud Purchase Act to Melbourne ; and
I venture to say that there is more valuable
information in that Act about “ money
for farmers,” than the Banking Commission
will ever collect from the high-salaried nonen-
tities they are examining. Balfour’s Act
modified would suit this province. The financ-
ing of the measure was done by Goschen,
and it was approved by Gladstone and all the
other authorities on finance in the House of
Commons. The objections to the Act from
the Irish tenants’ point of view were
that it was not made compulsory on
the landlords to sell, and consequently
the benefits it conferred on tenants were
confined to those whose landlords did actually
sell under it. Under the Act, £33,000,000 was
made available for the purpose of buying out
Irish landlords. The money was borrowed at
2| per cent, and lent to tenants at 4 per cent.
Of the difference of 1£ per cent., 1 per cent, was
put into a sinking-fund to pay off the whole
debt in 49 years, and the remaining J per cent,
was put into an insurance-fund to provide
against bad debts. The Vic. Government could
get money at per cent, and could lend it at
5 per cent. Of the 1J per cent, difference, 1
per cent, could go into a sinking-fund, to pro-
vide for paying off the principal, and the
remaining \ per cent, would be ample for the
purpose of meeting expenses and establishing a
fund to provide against losses, provided the
business were given over to a couple of com-
petent managers, who would be made directly
responsible for the administration of the scheme.
To my knowledge, Balfour’s Act has been a
success in Ireland wherever it was put in
operation. It should be signally successful in
Victoria. In conclusion, I shall only point out
that selectors in the hands of mortgagees see
before them no hope of ever paying off the prin-
cipal, or even continuing to meet the exorbitant
interest, consequently they are despondent.
Give them an opportunity through this scheme,
and despondency will yield to hope and courage.
The result will be such an impetus to produc-
tion, and to business generally, that deficits
will disappear and solid prosperity reign.—
Benalla.
Premier Turner’s Programme.
Next session the Victorian Government pro-
poses to attack three important reforms, and if
they can be effected the Turner Ministry will
have deserved well of the country, and the
Premier, if ejected at next general election,
can go back to the police courts and defend the
agile horse-thief with a light heart. A new
Companies Act is to be introduced which will,
it is alleged, make it impossible in the future
for “ unscrupulous directors and others to rob
the public.” If the Act is wide enough and
brainy enough to effect this purpose, it will be
the greatest reform which Victoria has enjoyed
for many years. Premier Turner is a serious,
practical man with much hard sense and
an immense capacity for detail, and he is
probably better qualified to draft the sort
of measure required than any other Australian
politician now in office. Whether he has
courage enough to do the work properly, or
whether he really wants to do it properly, or
whether the Legislature will allow him to do it,
will probably be known within the next three
months. In any case, the Bill is interesting as
an official acknowledgment that the Victorian
public has been robbed in the past by unscrupu-
lous directors to an extent which calls for
strong legislative measures, and is an indirect
acknowledgment that a good many people
ought to be in gaol who aren’t there. The
Purification of Rolls Act, which has served
no purpose worth mentioning except to
cause the wholesale disfranchisement of
voters principally Democratic voters is
to be abolished, and in its stead
the Government has devised u hat it believes to
be the simplest and most equitable system of
registration yet adopted in Australia. Con-
sidering that the Turner Government is largely
dependent on the Democratic vote this measure
will probably be shoved along with all the force
of the Ministerial party. Also a bill is to be
introduced compulsorily extending the blessings
of the Eight-hour system to the Chinese furni-
ture-maker—the leper who now works with
hands and feet and teeth for 70 hours a week,
and thereby turns out his goods at a rate
which has driven the white cabinet-maker out
of the trade. Other measures promised are a
new Insolvency Act which, it is hoped, will
exercise a salutary influence on the fraudulent
trustee, and the friendly bogus debtor, and the
man who secretly compounds for one fink in
the £; and a Sugar Guarantee Act, on the
Queensland system, to encourage the local beet-
industry. It is not, on the whole, a very large
programme, though it is quite large enough ;
and if these five proposals get safely through,
and are anything like up to the alleged sample,
the Turner Government will deserve another
year’s trial, anyhow.
“ Within the Meaning of the Act.”
The decision of the New South Wales Full
Court in a recent liquor-adulteration case is
calculated to fill the public with amazement at
the law’s boundless capacity for keeping clear
of anything resembling ordinary horse-sense.
In this instance, a police-inspector bought and
paid for a flask of brandy in the usual fashion,
and the liquor, on analysis, proved to be such
a fraudulent compound that a bench of
magistrates promptly fined the vendor .£lO.
Then that person (was the Licensed Victuallers’
Association behind him ?) appealed, and the
result of his appeal is one more proof that the
law can find a way to make an ass of itself out
of the most unpromising materials. The Full
Court decided that a sale to an inspector for
purposes of analysis wasn’t a sale “within the
meaning of the Act.” The law provides for the
punishment of anyone selling bogus liquor, but
the Court holds, in effect, that a sale, to be
a sale, must mean disposing of the liquor to
some one who intends using it for ordinary
drinking purposes. Under this ruling it will,
of course, be practically impossible to show
that the liquor was bought for actual drinking
purposes, unless the buyer proves his honajidets
by drinking it, and when he has drunk the
liquor it is beyond the reach of analysis. In
short, the law, as now interpreted, stands
somewhat as follows : It is provided by the
statute that anyone selling bogus liquor shall
be punished. An officer is appointed for the
purpose of sampling and inspecting liquor.
This officer, however, if he is to do any good in
his calling, must not buy any liquor himself for
purposes of analysis, nor must he send anyone
else to buy it. In fact, it must not be bought
for the purpose for which it is intended at all.
If the liquor is bought for analysis then
the seller can’t be prosecuted. The only
chance, therefore, is to wait until sohie
casual person, who bought liquor for
drinking purposes only, is so roused
to fury by the unspeakable quality of the drink
that he will lodge a complaint. As very few
people know good liquor from bad, a sufficiently
casual person will not be met with more than
once in 12 months or so—perhaps once in 20
years. When he is met with, it must happen
that he didn’t swallow his drink on the spot,
but took it away with him. Also, that he took
it away with a pure mind and a guileless soul—
if he swallows one poisonous “ nip ” at the
counter, and its quality makes him buy and
take away with him another for purposes of
further inspection, then the latter transaction
is not a sale “ within the meaning of the Act.”
Further that, having in a perfectly casual
manner, and with no after-intent, pur-
chased and taken away the liquor, with-
out sealing it up, or having brought
anyone with him for the purposes of testifying to
the purchase, he must still be able to prove that
it is the same liquor which he bought, or that
he bought any liquor at all. Also he must be
able to Drove that he was wholly and entirely
casual all through the transaction. Not one
purchaser in 100,000 can legally prove anything
of the kind, so that if the amazing decision of
the Full Court holds good it is hardly likely
that any more sellers of poisonous liquor will be
convicted in New South Wales. Under these
circumstances, The Bulletin desires to ask
the aforesaid Full Court one question : The
inspector asked for a flask of liquor ; it was
handed over to him and paid for in the ordinary
course; and the Court—the sensible Judge
Innes alone dissenting—has decided that
the transaction wasn’t a “sale.” Then what
was it, and (another pertinent question) wherein
was any injustice done to the publican by the
manner in which he was induced to part with
his liquor? The Supreme Court decision gives
a license-to-poiaon to every ruffian included in
the roll of N.S.W. licensed publicans; and
further handicaps the decent grog-vendors still
stumping along the uphill track of honesty.
One reason assigned by New South Wales Full
Court for its astonishing decision in the recent
Sydney drink-adulteration case was that the
Act didn’t define the meaning of the word
“sale.” If the Court applied this same
learned but ineffably stupid system all round,
nobody would ever be convicted of anything.
For instance, a man is hanged for wilfully
causing the death of another man ; will the
Full Court discharge every convicted murderer
until some one fixes up, in the criminal
code, an accurate definition of death, which
nobody has ever yet been able to define?
When the Land-tax comes into force, can
it be legally collected, according to the Full
Court, unless the Act accurately defines
the meaning of the word “laud?” There is no
possible definition except that land is a sub-
stance, whose component parts have been
elaborately ticketed by chemistry, and these
parts are supposed to have come from some
place not ascertained, and to have been made
out of something or nothing, at an undiscover-
able date, by some power whose address isn’t
on record, and which is best known by the fact
that many people have been killed for not
holding the same views concerning it as many
other people. Even if the Adulteration Act
did define the meaning of a “sale” beyond
all question, would things be much further
advanced, according to the logic of the
Full Court, unless there was also a suffi-
cient definition of an Act, so that the
Full Court could be dead-sure that the Adultera-
tion Act was an Act at all ? That Act is pre-
sumably a certain decision, arrived at according
to certain forms, by the men of New South
Wales, and New South Wales is a certain piece
of an undefinable substance called land, and no
one knows what man is, except that his niaterial
part is about 141 b. of ash and five buckets of
water, and that Mrs. Mellon attempted to
raise his spiritual or terial or hazy or gaseous
or invisible part, or the part which isn’t
shoved into the ground when he is dead, in
the presence of Judge Windeyer, and made a
very unconvincing display in consequence. The
mo'ral of all which is that no one can define
anything except by saying that it is made
directly or indirectly out of something else
which is undefinable, and if the Full Court is
going to follow its recent decision to its logical
conclusion it can’t even define itself sufficiently
well to enable it to do any business whatever.
A little more ordinary common sense and a
little less definition would do a lot to expedite
law proceedings, not only in Australia, but in
most other places besides.
In Melb. Supreme' Court, last week, Justice
Williams considered the action of a lower
Court in dismissing an information against one
Michael Healy. Bookmaker Healy had been
charged with using the Victorian Club, Melb.,
for the purpose of betting, contrary to section
51 of the Police Offences Act. Two inform-
ants called at the club, and asked for Mr.
Healy, who came out on the staircase and
booked wagers with them, in defiance of section
51 ; yet the magistrates dismissed the case.
Justice Williams decided that the magis-
trates were right, and discharged the
order for a review of their judgment,
with costs. The Victorian Club, as far
as Justice Williams knows it, may be
a place where bookmakers meet for prayer. Its
notoriety as a betting establishment goes for
nothing. The press publication of Victorian
club odds doesn’t count. “ No evidence was
tendered to show that the place in question was
used as a place for betting, and it was for the
magistrates to say whether the mere fact that
the defendant made two bets there at wide
intervals of time was sufficient to constitute a
use of that place for betting purposes. He
agreed with the magistrates that it was
not. If it had been Droved that the
club was formed for betting purposes,
and that the defendant had used it on four or
jive occasions for those purposes, he would have
said the magistrates were wrong.” This sort of
law isn’t dealt out to the nominal tobacconist
who bets with a “ fizgig ” policeman in plain
clothes, remember. He gets lined at once.
Nobody proves that he opened his shop for
bookniaking purposes. The law takes quite a
common-sense view of section 51 until it is used
for the purpose of suppressing betting, rather
than prosecuting certain men for making bets.
Then the law invariably discovers that the in-
fringement of the Act isn’t proved. The law
doesn’t blush for its own humbug, either. It
merely winks its other eye at the ceiling and
murmurs, “dismissed with costs.” By the
way, who pays costs in this particular farce ?
The retrenching Government?
It was proposed, in the excitement of last week,
that among other baits to catcli New South
Wales distracted public opinion Parkes should,
in his wolf-hunger for office, dangle a promise
to reconsider the question of State aid to deno-
minational education. The proposal was not
adopted during the presentation of his censure
speech, and the prospects of advantage from
the “dangle” are sufficiently unsubstantial to
justify a prompt rejection of the hint; but to
Parkes no suggestion comes amiss, and once he
realises that the advocacy of State aid to edu-
cation will lift him a step up towards the
Premiership, his whole career warrants the
assumption that he will embrace the opportunity.
Thus he will complete another of those circles
which politicians who live long are so
given to describe. James Martin, David
Buchanan and Henry Parkes were the head
and front of the first Protectionist band in
New South Wales. Parkes is now, at 80 years
of age, practically back in the old fortress, a
few cobwebby verbal distinctions being all he
carries to cover his Freetrade nakedness. He
began life similarly as the advocate of denomi-
uatioualism ; he fought vigorously against the
James Greenwoods and other secularising
powers of his time, to finally adopt the sub-
stance of their platform and dethrone denomi-
nationalism ; and what principle of his life
now stands in the way of his trying to restore
denominationalism, under the new guise of
payment by results ? When those who want
this can guarantee Parkes a Parliamentary
majority of one, the history of the old man’s
life gives proof that he will be with them.
Dear Bulletin, —Your par. re Vic. married
telegraph-messenger “boys” reminds me that
there is a similar anomaly in the N.S. Wales
railway-service. In that department there is a
grade of junior or boy-porters supposed to be of
any age between 15 and 21, who are paid at a
rate varying between 2s. 6d. and ss. per day,
and are supposed to receive appointments as
adult porters on becoming 21. But lately there
have been few vacancies to be filled, and there
are a great many “ boys ” of 25 years awaiting
promotion. Many are married and have fami-
lies ; my mate is 24 and supports a wife and
two children on ss. per day. The anomaly
works greater injustice to the railway-men
than to the telegraph people. The junior
porter under 21 is not allowed to work signals
and is not criminally liable if his mistake leads
to loss of life ; the junior over 21 is both de-
partmentally and criminally liable. It is
too late to say the standard of living should
not be reduced—it has been reduced ; now you
should say how it can be raised.— Railway
man. N.S.W., 16th May, 1895.
S. M. Herald, writing of Prof. Morris’s Higin-
botham memoir, sneerfully and triumphantly
asks; “What has he (Higinbotham) done?”
Like all the world’s great teachers, he preached,
and practised what he preached. Only that
and nothing more He preached—preached
Democracy, and practised it ! He gave to
Australia the whole gospel of popular Govern-
ment, and his life was an object-lesson in demo-
cratic citizenship. Subtract him from the roll
of Australian political history, and what
remains is chiefly a bevy of titled time-servers.
To say he was unpractical is no depreciation ;
for, if Higinbotham was unpractical, then in-
corruptible honesty is also unpractical. But the
charge is not true, any more than it is true that
he was a failure. It was the Democracy that
failed. It drew back from his noble yet
realisable ideal. Even if he did fail in any-
thing. he knew
. . . How far high failure
O'erleaps the bounds of low success.
And the Democracy is immeasurably richer for
his failures than for the successes of the mob of
popular mischievous mediocrities. The prin-
ciples he expounded were those of eternal
truth ; his adherence to them, his whole per-
sonality, stands imperishably fixed, a model for
the honest to imitate ; a measure whereby to
estimate, to condemn and to humiliate the
political traitor, timeserver, pigmy and
poltroon.
They are preparing to welcome him. There
i s already a Lord Brassey Hotel in Swauston-st.,

Avery short season of “Dorothy” com-
menced at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday, when
Miss Nellie Stewart made another conspicuous
success in‘ the character of sweet Dorothy Bantam.
The play which is built around her has no very
remarkable features—it is a pleasant and tuneful
affair, and that’s about all—but Dorothy is a
loveable little maid, and the average audience is
mashed on her to thelast man. Miss Florence Young,
who improves every day, took her old part of Lydia
Hawthorne with more success than heretofore,
and when these two ladies and Miss Pollard were
turned on together to sing their trio, “ Be Wise
in Time,” the audience got as much solid joy for
its money as any audience could -expect. At the
same time, it looks like gross extravagance to turn
on Nellie Stewart and Florence Young at the same
time: it is very much like drinking out of two
bottles at once. Lurcher, in the solid flesh of
George Lauri, was wholly satisfactory ; and Wal-
lace Brownlow and Tapley may be set down as
ditto, the latter being the more ditto of the two.
The audience always regards Joseph with con-
sideration as the iiushand of Vi. Varley, even
when he has only to stand round and fix his bale-
ful eye on somebody, but his song, “Though born
a man of high degree,” would have evoked ap-
plause even if that charmer had rejected his suit
and married a man named Higgins. “The Vicar
of Bray” is on the bill till Friday, and “Ma Mie
Rosette ” follows.
* * * *
Frederic Yilliers, the war “special,” has
done his brief season in Sydney, and leaves a
pleasant impression behind him. The Centenary
Hall was crowded on the night of his opening
lecture, though that tall, thin building, which
looks as if it had not got enough to eat when it
was young, was not the best kind of structure for
the purpose. The pillars blocked the view of the
pictures considerably, and from some parts only
the gaiters of the lecturer were visible. Mr.
Yilliers lectured in his campaigning suit, instead
of in the Cimmerian tail-coat of an effete civilisa-
tion, and thereby economised his wash-bill con-
siderably. The costume was generally approved
of; even if he had lectured in his night-shirt it
would have been a pleasant change from the
conventional dicky and bone-stud. As far as
can be guessed from his utterances, Mr.
Yilliers is not a humorist on a large scale,
and he makes no pretence of being one.
He tells his plain, unvarnished tale in a
straightforward manner, and illustrates it with
many convincing views of Japanese and other
scenery, and though there is not much in his tale
that has not been heard already, yet the illustra-
tions compensate for any deficiencies. In his
opening lecture he only dropped into a mirthful
mood twice—once regarding a mule and once in
the matter of a dead horse. Also, he dropped
once into profound pathos when he described the
massacre at Port Arthur, and his pathetic voice
proved to be very like his humorous voice, and his
ordinary business voice was exactly the same as
both of them. Probably his utterance has lost its
flexibility through altercations with many camel-
drivers in different climes, and through arguing with
mules and dromedaries. Summed up, the famous
special is a useful and agreeable adjunct to the
pictures ; he is a wiry-looking man of no swearable
age, with a serviceable pair of legs, a pronounced
English drawl, a bright eye, and a habit of going
straight at his subject. He begins without any
dreadful preliminary joke about the beautiful har-
bour ; he is judiciously reticent about the great
people of his acquaintance, and doesn’t talk about
himself more than is necessary; and when he is
finished he goes straight away without any perora-
tion. Altogether, The Bulletin views Smythe’s
latest acquisition with feelings of respect.
* * * *
At Sydney Royal Miss Maggie Moore has
relapsed into Irish drama, and this week “ Arrah-
na-Pogue” and “ Colleen Bawn ” have been turn-
ing up alternately. The man doesn’t live who
could say anything new about these hoary dramas,
and the only thing which is visibly new about the
cast is that Mr. H. R. Roberts is steadily develop-
ing a rich and powerful brogue, and that Mr.
West is about the most comprehensively villain-
ous-looking informer on record. A week of
“ Struck Oil ” comes next, and on June Ist the
Irish element reappears with “Sweet Innisfail.”
The present, by the way, isn’t a good time for
Irish drama ; it should be put on in the summer,
when it is possible to spend a night behind a hedge
with a gun waiting for the landlord without catch-
ing rheumatism.
* * * *
Her Majesty’s also falls into Irish drama
on Saturday, when Mr. Charles Erin Verner,
“ the greatest Irish comedian of the age,” appears
in “ Shamus O’Brien.” This rush of Irish drama
is unaccountable.
* * * *
A grand new skating ballet has been added
to the programme at Sydney Tivoli, which pro-
gramme keeps expanding to such a degree that
some day the buttons will inevitably fly off at the
back. The other feature on the bill is that Harry
Rickards, and his wonderful hat, and his other
appurtenances, leave for London on June 10 in
search of new talent, and the date of his return is
uncertain.
* * * *
Another minstrel co,, under the manage-
ment of Messrs. York and Jones, has opened the
Opera House, and hopes, with the hope that
keeps alive despair, to run those roman-starred
premises successfully. The new co, is a
tolerably respectable affair, and if Sydney really
wants a second variety show, and the public can be
induced to climb up the interminable stairs of the
old house in York-street, and the programme
keeps up to the first night’s standard, all may be
well with the venture.
Melbourne’s theatrical carnival, inaugur-
ated last year at a muddy crioket-ground, is to
take place in the Exhibition building this time.
Wednesday next is the appointed day, and the
programme prepared for the occasion carries its
own recommendation with it, apart from charit-
able considerations. The public are not humbly
petitioned to assist distressed actors. They are
merely invited to patronise a huge show provided
by professional brethren of the distressed ones
aforesaid. It may be mentioned, all the same,
that the profits of these bona fide benefits are
devoted to chanty, and not to “organisation.”
The cost of distributing the cash is less than one
per cent., and of every shilling paid for a ticket of
admission something like lid. goes to a good
cause.
* * * *
The Melbourne theatres have mostly been
content to go on and prosper, as before, since
they were last observed by The Bulletin. “ In
Town ” is still crowding the Princess’s, but the
charm of that stupendous success was lessened on
Saturday night by the absence, through illness, of
Charles Ryley. The Duke of Muffshire who ap-
peared in Ryley’s stead was a hard, unsympathetic
duke, and when he contributed a touch of senti-
ment the show seemed to recoil, as though shrink-
ing from contact with a cold foot. “ In Town ”
will end on Friday, and “The Shop Girl,” with
Harry Monkhouse in a leading part, is due upon
the following night. “ The Shop Girl,” according
to present threats, can only run for one consecu-
tive week, after which George Edwardes’s com-
pany—whose all-round ability becomes more glar-
ingly apparent every night—will leave Melbourne,
never, or hardly ever, to return.
“ The Span of Life ” continues to bridge
the chasm of weekly exes, at Melb. Royal, and
manager Bland Holt has no weak ambition to put
up another drama before his tuft of profit on the
Saturday side of the precipice has been fairly worn
away by grabbing at it. The baby camel fell sick
recently, whereupon baby’s humpy parent ob-
jected to crossing the desert alone, and tried to
shove a few Arab sheiks into the orchestra. Other-
wise, the course of Bland Holt’s panorama has
been running smooth.
** * *
The Rickards Cos. warbles to packed
benches. Two American artists, Murphy and
Mack, are to make their first Australian appear-
ance at the Opera House on Saturday, when the
surplus audience will probably require to be
driven back with hand-spikes. A. hand-spike at
this establishment is the only possible alternative
to a summons for overcrowding. Frank Clark s
variety show at the Bijou had a fairly spccessful
first week, and the programme is improving
steadily. Also the Cambridge has struck another
double bill which is distinctly than the
previous effort, and D’Arcy Stanfield’s intelligent
humor gets two good opportunities to score.
** * *
Clifford Halid, son of Sir Charles of that
ilk, will deliver lectures upon the art of singing,
at Melb. Athenaeum Hall, on four evenings next
week. Mr. Halid’s grip of his subject is said to
be strong, and, as an entertainer, he brings a
reputation from India, together with a lady
vocalist, of whom more anon. Just now, when
every second damsel in Melbourne is training her
squeak with a view to European triumphs, a word
of wisdom from a Halle should be very welcome.
** * *
Percy Grainger, the 12-year-old pianist>
has been packed off to Germany by his Melbourne
friends. He gave an astonishing farewell per-
formance at the Town Hall (where the ladies
bombarded him with chrysanthemums), and left no
room for doubt that he ought to make a stir in the
world hereafter. Percy is by this time a painful
little prig. Most spoilt boys of his class require an
occasional basting to keep them up to the mark.
Having found out that they are clever they don’t
want to learn any more. Child Percy regards
himself as a marvellously gifted father to the man
Grainger, who must stick close to his music if he
intends to give his offspring a good start in life.
That’s genius of a sort. Juvenile faith is the
future reward of industry. One smirking youngster
thinks “ I’m a bright boy,” and stops at that ;
but the other kid thinks —“ What shall Ibe like
presently if I practise my talents eight hours a
day!” Percy Grainger would practise himself to
death if his* mother did not lure him from the
piano at stated times, and induce him to study his
health. Then he rushes out into the fresh air to
gain the strength necessary for thumping the keys
some more. With luck he may become another
Paderewski.
At Melbourne Exhibition, on the twenty-ninth of
May,
Good pros, of all degrees
Have volunteered to please
The pleasure-seeking public, who can well afford
to pay
A coin of modest worth
For the “ biggest show on earth.”
A band of choice musicians, under Marshall Hall,
will play
Selections through the day,
Whilst other musio-hall-ers lend a willing
Aid, with songs and dances duly stated in the list.
Then, underneath the dome
The newest stars from ’Ome
With recitations, comedies, and sketches will
assist
To keep the game alive,
And Ouda takes a dive
From the roof into a netting (just imagine—if he
missed !) —
And the growlers who insist
On getting ample value for a shilling,
Will not be disappointed if a moment they can
snatch
To see the fun outside,
Where the damsels sit astride
Their patent safety bicycles, and scud away from
scratch,
Or otherwise compete
In races short and sweet.
Not least of the attractions is a costume football
match ;
Apart from these a batch
Of entertainments humorous or thrilling,
And a sale of dolls at auction to delight the kiddies
small,
Will help to fill the bill.
Down, flutterer ! Be still
Whilst we speak about a matter which is richest
of them all
In the rapture it affords,
“The Beauties of the Boards ”
Have agreed to sell refreshments in a perfume-
laden hall.
O, when their glances fall
On a johnnie the effect is very killing.
Ten thousand hearts are craving for admission to
a tent
Where angels fair and bright,
Ever gracious and polite
To the most suspicious matron, or the baldest
headed gent,
Will sweeten cup of tea,
In the cause of charitee,
By smiling at the “ sugar ” which a customer has
spent.
And this glorious event
Will be offered to the public at a shilling—
Vide billing.
On the 29th of May
This remarkable array
Of talent will do honor to the actor old and gray,
Of a long-forgotten day,
Also younger ones who cannot keep the yelping
wolf away.
And a ticket may be purchased for a shilling.
At Poverty Point.
Dear Bulletin,— Re a recent par. Melb. bald-heads,
discussing Gaietv Girlhood, may plump for Maud Hobson
(who isn’t plump, but bonily-large) ; johnniedom may
elect the Palotta ; the “ gods ” may vote for Florence
Lloyd ; d.c. (which seldom stands for “ discerning")
maidens may admire Blanched Massey and Decimal
Moore; but all have failed to discover the finest girl of
the imported crowd. For symmetrical, upstanding figure,
shapely—er—continuations of the foot, pretty face and
sparkling expression, Ethel Carlton, boss nymph of the
chorus, and “ the housekeeper ” in the first act of
“In Town,” is first past the post, and all pro-
tests are dismissed. She’s understudy for more
than one of the principals, and if she gets the chance
to linger for more than a few too transient seconds on the
stage, the great combined heart of Melbourne’s bald-
headedness, johnniedom, “gods,” and d.c. maidenhood
will rise and sing the name of “ Ethel" down the ringing
grooves of artistic appreciation. She wears pink tights
in the greenroom of “In Town,” and already one prin-
cipal lady has gone back into skirts, while another would
also hide her “ pillars ” were she not representing a male
character at the “Ambiguity.” If Miss Carlton were to
play Tybalt for one night, that bit of sword rehearsal
might be serious enough to justify the horrified inter-
vention of the Rev. Leedham Bantock Hopkins.—Yours,
Bull’s Eye. [The Bulletin hopes that the gentleman,
having got this off his chest, now feels much better. The

said lady in pink tights, is now prepared to hear learned

At “In Town,” the other evening, a “fashionable”
party occupied a box. The behaviour of the ladies was
so extraordinary that it almost interfered with the per-
formance. They laughed and talked loudly and made
signs to the actors with their handkerchiefs. At last the
amphitheatre was moved to wrath, and requested loudly
that the party should be “chucked out.” Things are
hardly less bad in the dress-circle of Sydney Lyceum,
where, especially on first nights, the cackle of a number
of well-dressed people is absolutely unendurable—yet has
to be endured. But why has it?

That charming Melb. girl, Lalla Miranda, studying
under Madame Richard, made her dMmt in Paris, singing
from Gounod’s “ Romeo et Juliette." European critics
credit her with the possession of remarkable quality and
clearness of voice,'likening it to the true nightingale
tones of the Van Zandt and Melba order. Continental
connoisseurs pronounced the “ glistening-eyed ” a beauty;
so, doubtless, with such hall-marks of approval she will
one day stand on a high rung of the operatic ladder.

The Wild-Cat Column .
Among his other “economies,” Premier Reid
promises to save £234,000 tor the year by knocking £25,000
off the interest on the public debt; reducing the annual
payment in redemption of the railway loan of 1867 from
£70,000 to £15,000 ; reducing the payments in redemption
of Treasury bills from £206,000 to £103,000 ; reducing the
payments on account of another railway loan from £75,000
to £37,500; and cutting down the naval tribute from
£37,000 to £24,000. The £25,000 off the public debt can
only be saved by a conversion operation, which is not yet
effected and which may not be successful, or by default-
ing ; and all the other “savings” can only be managed
by special Acts of Parliament, which Acts may not pass.
Moreover, £196,000 of the amount is not a saving at all,
but a mere shuffling of to-day’s liabilities on to posterity.
Most of Premier Reid's finance consists of shuffling the
burden on to posterity ; the balance is a simple process of
counting the fowls before the eggs are hatched.
V ♦ ♦ ♦
The Royal Bank of Queensland, like several
other reconstructed institutions, is considering a scheme
for further re-arrangement of its affairs. The Royal
settled with its depositors in ’93 by issuing deposit re-
ceipts at 4J per cent., payable by instalments in ’9B, ’99,
1900 and 1901. Since then it has released all its current
accounts, repaid one-fourth of its Govt, deposits, and
one-half of the ’9B instalment of its ordinary deposits,
and it is now about to repay the second half of the
game instalment. In addition to this, its business
has shown remarkable steadiness in the last two much-
depressed years, and, besides paying regular divs., it
has made considerable additions to its reserve fund.
It now proposes to raise money by an issue of 4 per
cent, debentures, and pay off its remaining 4£ per
cent* deposits. This will make a considerable saving of
interest—probably to the extent of £3OOO per annum.
The money is to be obtained in London—wherein the un-
speakable pig-headedness of even the intelligent variety
of Australian financier glares forth conspicuously. If the
Royal of Queensland can raise money at 4 per cent, in
London, where it is but little known, it could raise it
quite as easily, and at the same rate, in Australia, where
is has earned the reputation of being—with the exception
of the Commercial of Sydney—the soundest and most
promising of the reconstructed banks, and where its
repute stands higher than that of one or two banks which
survived the ’93 crisis. There is so much money in
Australia crying aloud for some kind of reasonably safe
investment, that this slavish adherence to the old custom
of relying on British capital is unaccountable.
if the Royal of Queensland gets its money in
London at 4 per cent., on its own security, how will that
transaction look by which the B.N.Z. gave the same rate
of interest for its £2,000,000 with the guarantee of the
State thrown in ? The combined guarantee of the pro-
vince and the B.N.Z. was certainly a little better than
that of the province alone, and as Maoriland can float its
own loans at a good deal less than 3J per cent., that
famous £2,000,000 transaction now looks a most luckless
affair.
♦ ♦ * *
At the National Bank meeting, it was feelingly
remarked by the chairman -according to the newspaper
report—that “ the interest due and payable on deposits
for the past half-year was £IBO,OOO, which absorbed the
treater portion of the profits, and it would be a great
relief if a reduction in the rate of interest could now be
made.” This may, or may not, mean that the National
is beginning to regard it as a far-off possibility that it may
have to appeal to its depositors—after the manner of two
other Vic. institutions —for a reduction of interest, but if
the steady drop in the profits continues, it is evident that
a time will come when the National, like every other bank
in like circumstances, must do something. And an appeal
to the depositors would be the most probable device
under the circumstances. A reduction of even 10s. per
cent, would save £40,000 per annum, which would be a
material assistance. Tub Bulletin can lay its finger on
at least four other banks which will most likely make the
smie appeal for consideration by-and-hye. The number
of banks hasn’t materially diminished, those that died
being mostly institutions which did very little genuine
banking. The amount of business to be done, however,
has decreased probably 30 per cent, in the last few years ;
the rate of interest has fallen so much that this decline
means a drop of something like 60 per cent, in profits ;
and meanwhile the rate payable on deposits—owing to
the cast-iron reconstruction policy—has rather increased
than diminished. Every Australian “reconstruct” save
two shows a general downward tendency in profits, and
unless there is a big general revival there must some day
be a stampede for a second reconstruction which will be
not quite so unanimous as the stampede of 1893, but still
quite unanimous enough.
