Melbourne papers mention joyfully that the
reduction of Savings Bank interest to 3 and 2 per cent, is
driving money out of that institution at the rate of about
BCOO per week, and forcing it into “ other channels of
investment.” Considering that the P.O. Savings Bank
could give the old 4 per cent, and still pay its way, the
reduction is an undisguised attempt to bulldoze the
working classes into trusting their money once more to
the old boom financiers, or else it is an effort to make
them buy up Murphy’s tenements at Elsteruwick, now in
the possession of Mirams’ old building society. In other
words, the Turner Government is restoring confidence
with a club.
• * • ♦
The balance-sheet of the Bank of Australasia
appears in the advertising columns of this issue. The
net profit for the half-year ended 15th Oct, 94, is £45,734,
against £60,606 for the similar half-year of ’93. The
div., which was as high as 10 per cent, in 92, has gob
down by a steady descent to 6 per cent., and even that
absorbs £48,000, or £2266 more than the actual profit for
the six months, then by reducing the balance to credit of
profit and loss by that amount. Said balance is now
only £9641. The Australasia has made no addition to its
reserve fund (£800,000) since ’BB—the good old times of
12 per cent. div. Comparing its present position with
lhat of April ’9O, its note ciroulition has decreased by
£87,000 ; its d-posits by £700,000; its bills payable, &c.,
by nearly £390,000; miscellaneous advances by over
£1,700,000; investments by £270,000; loans at call and
short notice by £360,000 ; and the amount available for
distribution has fallen from £127,000 to £57,000. The
only important increase is in “cash on band, which
is £1,100,000 more than in ’9O. Considering that there
have been no reconstruction troubles in the case, these
figures—especially when compared with those of the
Union and B.N.S.'W.— suggest thatthissolidold institution
is suffering from too much conservatism in its business
methods.
♦ ♦ * *
The Equitable Permanent Benefit, Building,
Land and Savings Institution of Sydney (ye gods, what a
name !) shows what can be done, even in the worst times,
by a society which never attempts to bite off more than it
can conveniently chew. Up to date it has met all
demands; it has £107,000 of deposits and has refused
many more for want of means of investment; it has a
reserve-fund of £21,000, a contingency fund of £2055,
and, after paying its regular rate of interest on deposits
and shares, has £2500 profit on the year’s transactions.
Its assets, amounting to £214,000, are free from ail mort-
gage and encumbrance, and it has a bank balance of
£19,931, and £SOOO in N.S.W. Government securities. The
pity is that more building societies are not run on the
same careful lines.
« * * *
The Vie. butter and cheese trade is in a state
approaching insurrection. According to a contract
entered inta by the Patterson Govt., the Agricultural
Department is bound to send all its shipments of butter
and cheese by the steamers of the two mail companies
only. The freight agreed upon is nearly 30 per cent,
higher than present current rates ; the companies are not
absolutely bound to find accommodation for all ship-
ments, and as the cargo which they fail to find space for
can’t, under the contract, be sent by other vessels, it must
deteriorate and accumulate charges in the stores until the
next opportunity for shipment; and the mail boats carry
to London only, so that the consignments (or Glasgow,
Liverpool and other ports have to bo transhipped and
additional freights incurred. This last provision makes
the Vic. farmer particular wrathy, as he might, but for
the contract, send his goods direct to these ports at much
lower freights than he now pays to London only. Many
tons of produce have gone bad on his hands through this
clumsy arrangement, and when its author next appeais to
the electors the smell thereof will rise up against him
like the accusing ghost of much lost butter come to
judgment.
* * » *
The keynote of the success of our export food
trade is flavour ; without proper study of this attribute
all other efforts will be vain. Feeding for flavour is
almost a science in Europe, and the best is selected for
export, whilst the remainder goes into home consumption.

The public debt of N.S.W. on 31st December,
1894, was £58,204,253, and the annual interest thereon
£2,205,620. The cheerful fact is also mentioned that the
province has lost over £3,100,000 in discounts, expenses of
floating loans, etc., and pays about £IIO,OOO interest on
this sum which it didn’t get, but which it will have to pay
back all the same.
♦ ♦ * *
The Australian Mutual Fire Insurance Co.’s
report shows that the premium income for the year end-
ing 31st March, 1895, was £29,416, and the yield from in-
vestments £5797; total, £35,213. The total profit was
£16,765, of which £4OOO goes to reserve, making it
£86,000 ; £2250 to contingency fund, making it £14,000 ;
£2OOO to shareholders ; and £7723 as bonus divided
between shareholders and policyholders. The direc-
tors have also resolved to capitalise £12,500 of the
contingency fund, which will raise the capital to
£25,000. This ingenious method of doubling the
capital will enable the shareholders in future to get as
much profit out of an 8 per cent. div. as they formerly did
out of one of 16 per cent., and 8 per cent, isn’t nearly so
suggestive of the bloated and grasping capitalist as the
other thing. Altogether, the Australian Mutual Fire Cos.
can pride itself on being a prosperous and remarkably
well-managed affair.
* ♦ » ♦
It is cabled that the City of Melbourne Bank
depositors decline to grant any reduction of interest
“until a full enquiry has been made into the affairs of
the bank.” Tub Bullbtin’s personal opinion is that, with
a reduction of deposit interest to 3 per cent., and no
inquiry whatever, the City has a good chance to
get through, and to become, many years hence, a
tolerably flourishing concern; but that, with a full
inquiry, it will have very little chance of any kind.
The Bulletin has always advocated full inquiry, but this
time it is not talking of what should be, but of what—so
far as it can see—really is in this particular case, as well
as in several others. Many a patient would give up the
ghost if presented with a complete diagram of the state of
his liver and other internal organs, and yet has a
chance of recovery if the awful condition of his
“ innards” is not fully revealed to him. And very few
Victorian financial institutions have seen a correct
colored diagram of their own liver since 1890.

A year or two ago a British depositor in a
certain busted Australian bank wrote out to one of the
liquidators, asking for the history of a certain financier’s
companies. The liquidator clipped from a London
journal a column of particulars re Jabez Balfour and his
companies, wrote on a piece of paper—“ Here you have
the history of the companies,” pinned the paper to
the clipping, and posted to the anxious enquirer. One of
these days, perhaps, the liquidator will receive from
England an account of Balfour’s trial, with sarcastic
comments attached.

The writer has just been shown a sample ship-
ment. of combs, tooth, hair and other brushes, purses,
hand-bags, &0., all made in Japan, and all stamped
“ First quality. Paris.” Not only are the goods
splendidly finished; but they cost betweenlOperoent. and
20 per cent, less than goods of the same quality hitherto
imported from France and Germany. ’Ome manu-
facturers who allow the Jap. to get a footing will have a
hard job to oust him. How is it, though, that goods
made in Japan, but marked as of Paris make, are allowed
to pass the Vic. Customs ?
r * • • *
It’s quite the rule when a man assigns his estate
and pays, say, 3s. in the £, for his “ friends” or his wife to
come forward and buy the estate back from the assignee.
It’s only in Australia, apparently, that so many friends
who would not save a man from bankruptcy rush forward
enthusiastically to help him after he has gone bung. They
never kick a man when he’s down. Perhaps their busi-
ness experience teaches them that the man who pays 3s.
in the £ eventually dies worth a pile.

Death from accidents and violence continue very
large among the A.M.P. assurers, according to the official
report Of the 848 claims of the year (one through a
client being killed in the Matabele war), 119, or 14 per
cent., were due to these causes; and 40, or nearly 5 per
cent! occurred in the first year of assurance.

A N.Q. broker and the head of the local tele-
graph-office have been caught at the old game—standing
in to profit by private wires. Tbe broker has been dismissed
the Exchange, and the telegraph-man fined £so—which,

mining-man declared that the shares of a Murchison
show he had recently placed in London had jumped to a
big premium, and offered to take 5000 scrip at par. One
of the original vendors accepted, and next day called
round with the scrip for the money. But instead of cash
he got: “Veil, veil—l vas only booming the mine ; did
you not see me vink ? ”

The halt-ton of gold a month output from Went-
worth mine, N.S.W., came like angels’ visits. Expected
this month, 12,000 oz.; received, a little over 2000 oz.
Shares went up like a rocket six weeks ago to £2, and
came down like a stick below par when this awful slump
wag known last week.

George Smith, founder, if not actual finder, of
Baker’s Creek, who made a pile out of the mine and
lost it in other businesses he less understood, is now
manager of Baker’s Creek Consolidated—now called
Baker’s Creek Proprietary.
• * ♦ •
The pluck, enterprise and patience of Parkes
miners are being hummed in Sydney papers. Where
would it all be but for the Bushman’s tribute, out of
which a party of miners from Ironbarks and elsewhere
made a few thousands, encouraging many others to try
and do likewise, which, in all probability, they won t?
NSW. shareholders in the Central mine seem
opposed to its sale for £175,000. A few months ago it
could have been bought for as many shillings, and no im-
portant developments have occurred in the mine since.
Prospi cts of bi-metallism and the economical solution of
the sulphide problem have prompted the offer. One is as
remote as the other, and the shareholders should enlarge
the deposit and decrease the time of offer, or the chances
are they may get left with a mine without divs.

E. W. Hawker, M.P., an Adelaide director of
the Central 8.H., publicly writes that the “ New York ”
syndicate which is after the Central is interested in Ash-
croft’s sulphide process. It is surmised now
York ”is another way of spelling “Queen-street.” But
the Central’s white lead is an attraction as well as the
sulphides. The big mine wants the lead badly : it is
being raked now ior carbonates.
* » » •
Even Junction North participated in the mild
boom which began at the middle of the month. Thou-
sands of these shares were forfeitable for non-payment of
calls, but when (for some reason not very apparent) the
stock jumped up, the mine authorities were implored to
accept back calls. They did, stipulating only that shares
so dealt with should be sent to the office for registration
in the names of present holders. Thus future collection
of calls will be assisted—and a great deal of stock con-
\eniently kept off the market for a week or ten days.
Hancock’s sulphide-jigging patent is being
pushed in Adelaide ; the local Advertiser helping.
Hancock has followed Ashcroft (his strongest rival) to
England Greenway, Block 14 metallurgist, seems to he
one of the chief barrackers for the Hancock process.
... *
The famous sulphides dump at Block 10 is quite
a useful institution. On Ist Oct., ’94, it was esti-
mated to contain 39,734 tons of ore valued at £19,668 10s.
During the ensuing half-year the operations of the mine
threatened to result in a loss, hut the dump was equal tp
the occasion. Although it had meanwhile been reduced
by 1540 tons it nevertheless (by reason of cheaper pro-
cesses ’) obligingly increased in value to £28,645105. This
magnanimous conduct of the dump enabled the co.
to announce in the last half-yearly report a “balance
carried down, being net profit earned during the half-
year ” of £7462 7s. lid.
The Central Cos., in purchasing the Extended
property for £ISOO, got good value in machinery. The
mine will not be used other than as a means of ventila-
tion by connection with the lower Central workings.
Junction Cos. winds up its last half-year (to
March 31) with a working account credit balance of
£422 7s. 4d. Metropolitan management expenses (£464
8s 3d )’transform this, in the profit and loss account,
into a debit of £42 os. lid. Comparison is favorable with
the previous half-year, which showed a loss of £1921
10s. 9d.—of which £9Bl 10s. accrued on the accounts of
the period before that.
The Junction suspended smelting at Port
Adelaide in February, and the new concentrating plant
is now at work at the mine. Results to date are hopeful.
The plant has a capacity of 150 tons a day, and will he
extended considerably if results are satisfactory. The
slimes are to be treated by May’s patent separators.
* * * *
Block 10 had its most successful concentration
last week. The mill put through 308 tons, and recovered
731 per cent, of the lead, and nearly 52 per cent, of the
silver contents. But this “record” was due rather to
the ore than to any improvement made in the mill. The
ore dealt with, by the way, is very like the Junction sul-
phides which are, on the whole, as easy to treat as any-
thing on the field ; so that, after all, even the patched-up
plant lately put on Junction may do.
Amos and Mills have secured a lease 15 miles
from Tuena N.S.W. It would be more interesting to
know what has become of the Tuena mine, floated in
Sydney last year, in which some rich calcite specimens
played an important part.
Says a S.M.H. wire; “Whelan and Hearn-
shaw are progressing favorably,” by which it would
appear they were in the hospital. Worse luck, it means
they are trying to find a rich reef at Bywong.

Lime has just been struck in the Gin Gin dis-
trict (Bundaberg, Q.). It is found on the surface in large
quantities sufficient to meet the requirements of the
sugar industry for many years. Distance from Bunda-
berg only 36 miles, whereas all lime is at present brough'
from Gympie, a distance of 107 miles.

Alan Bright’s Circular gives Charters
Towers April figures 21,310 tons crushed for 20,9440 z.,
an increase of 1597 tons and 9270 z. as compared
with the previous April. Dividends, £21,533 ; calls,
£6384. For the four months the divs. are £89,712,
as compared with £73,738 for same period of 94 ,
calls so far are £23,980, or £6924 in excess of same period
last year. Excess of divs. over calls for the four months
is £65,732, or just £9OOO better than for the first third of
’94. The field has half a million pounds’ worth of machi-
nery on it, and employs over 3500 men. Charters Towers
will lead Australian goldfields for many years to come.
The outside shows are not developing too well, but inside
the magic horseshoe the productiveness appears almost
inexhaustible. Mills United stone shows a decline.

The “ Triumph ” puddler and concentrator,
elsewhere advertised, should repay inspection by mining
men. It is claimed that this machine puts quantity
through more cheaply than any machine on the market;
also that the parts are simple, and not liable to get out
of order.

A competent, but unbusinesslike, miner was
recently appointed at a handsome salary to take charge
of an English alleged mine in Westralia. Examining the
property he satisfied himself beyond a doubt it was a
duffer, and, without formally resigning or saying a word,
shouldered his swag and walked off. His only explana-
Hon (sufficient in every other business but mining) was
“The and thing is no good.” However, the mine
remained unjumped in his absence—another proof of its
value. Yet shares are, or have been, at a premium in
London.

At the Londonderry shareholders’ meeting, after

Bayley’s has had another mild flutter—in fact,
they even went to 13s. one day at Coolgardie. Next we
shall hear of Coolgardieites buying and putting them
away for a year. Some wonderful strike is saidjto have
been made at 380 ft., and it is also announced that the
North-eastern shaft has struck it rich. But no corres
pondent considered it worth while to wire the last crush-
ing viz., 330 tons for 290nz.-under an ounce—which,
under ordinary conditions, can surely not pay at Cool-
gardie. It is equally well to remember that that mar-
vellous market-organiser, Sylvester Browne, is now in
London. “ Beware of the Bull.” This purely bucolic
notice ought to be posted in some ore-paddcoks.
The new find at Bayley’s this week was thrown
open to pressmen and others. One par. : “ The gold is
very heavy, coarse but free, and was right through the
stone.” “Coarse but free” is good, and evidences sn
enviable technical knowledge. Another par.; “The
shoot of gold is five inches wide, and the find is con-
sidered most important as proving permanency.” A five-
inch shoot and permanency is also good. Fiom these in-
dications The Bulletin gleans that whisky is still more
plentiful than water in Coolgardie mining-reporter circles.

The litigation in connection with the Prince of
Wales and Bonshaw Cos. (Ballarat) seems interminable.
There have already been four actions against the co. for
alleged illegal forfeiture of shares, several appeals to Vic.
FulfCourt—all dismissed—and there is now an appeal
pending to the Privy Council. In all these actions, the
co. has been treated as a registered co., but now some of
the litigants are starting new actions, in which they are
treating the co. as unregistered. Meanwhile, the co. is
afraid to declare any divs.

Because the jury had found that Dooley,
the Oollingwood murderer, killed his wife and
mother-in-law, also the lodger (The Bulletin has
a soft spot in its heart for the man who kills the
lodger) while sane, the assassin’s body was “buried
like a dog.” The “ funeral ” took place at night;
but why Dooley wasn’t planted at the intersection
of “four cross roads with a stake in his inside”
has not been explained. The jury’s verdict was,
of course, an insult to human intelligence. If
Dooley had simply suicided without killing any-
one else, the twelve bumpkins would, as usual,
have returned a verdict of “unsound mind ; ” but
as he committed a sensational series of offences
they went out of their way to say he was sane.
The man’s brain was actually diseased, but pro-
bably there were a number of worse idiots on the
]ory ‘A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback— the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

The horse owhied by Deeming, when in

To a Euroa (Vic.) couple was lately born
their 31st child. The father, mother and the 31
children are all living.
Monkhouse, the unctuous “Gaiety ” come-
dian, has Melbourne relatives in the undertaking
line. “ From gay to grave !”
The V.D.L. Bank lottery tenth prize, a
farm valued at .£750, goes to Graham Berry (a
Tender-board official), son of the Vic. Speaker.
Premier Turner is the shabbiest-looking
man in Vic. Parliament. Some of his Tory
opponents talk of subscribing to get him anew
hat.
The modest-living Premier Reid is the
richest N.S.W. Premier since Jennings, for even
if he has bub little money he is believed to be with-
out debts.
There are four Knights and two C.M.G.’s
in Adelaide whose aggregate possessions would
barely reach <£lo,ooo. None of them has ever
been insolvent.
David Syme, of the Age, owns a large
dairy-farm at Woori Yallock, near Lilydale. He
visits the place regularly every week, and “sees
that the coos are milked recht.”
Judge Real is rapidly qualifying as heavy
father of the Q. Bench. Platitudes galore roll
from his lips, and he chatters as much in Banco
as the other Judges and counsel combined.
Michael Davitt freely admits that he is
lecturing for his own private benefit, as his funds
are low and his health not good. He is to be run
by the Hibernian societies in each province.
The eldest son of John Downer, Q. 0.,
M.P. of S.A., who had begun a promising career
at one of the English universities, is being brought
back to Adelaide by his mother in sadly-broken
health.
Zox, the corpulent Hebrew Vic. M.P.,
slipped and fell the other day. When the man
who helped him up remarked that the pavements
were slippery with rain, Zox answered : “ No, my
man, it’s a heavy fall of Jew.”
Calcutta Sunday Times has joined the
ranks of the pirates. In its issue of 7th April
Edward Dyson’s powerful story, “ A Demon with
Women,” originally published in The Bulletin
some years ago, is reprinted as original.
In the course of a stormy meeting of the
Brighton (Melb.) Council last week, ex-Speaker
Bent said, “Many gentlemen, myself included,
could not take the mayoral chair without financial
assistance.” Poor old gentleman Bent!
A London solicitor is advertising for the
will or probate of the will of Christopher Webb,
who died in Sydney (N.S.W.) about 1820, and who
is believed to have bequeathed moneys to four
ladies named Sparke, then living in England.
Apropos the Tit-Bits literary-theft-system,
Meston has found someone else to take him seri-
ously. An old-time article of his recently ap-
peared in an English paper of the Field and
Garden type. Prize awarded to the successful
thief, 10 guineas.
H. H. Champion is a direct descendant,
through his mother, of that Sir Thomas Urquhart
who was the author of the most famous transla-
tion of “ Rabelais ” in the English language, and
who died of laughing when he heard of the restora-
tion of Charles 11.
The latest delusion of an Adelaide million-
aire is a dread of dying poor. He has developed a
craze for drawing large sums of money out of the
bank and biding them about the house in which
he lives. The doctors give him, at the outside,
only a year to live.

Payment of Members’ Act now engages N.S.W.
Full Court, was, for a time, on the editorial staff
of Westminster Review (London), and acted for
several years as adviser re purchases of pictures
for the N.S.W. Art Gallery.

What may not happen to a political giant
in Australia? An ex-member’s house was invaded
the other day by a friend of the family. The
visitor was courageously ushered into the kitchen
by the “missus.” The politician had his hands
and arms in the suds. It was washing-day.
William Brennan, the portly senior
messenger at the N.S.W. Colonial Secretary’s
office, has just retired after nearly half a century’s
service, during which time he has seen some 15
individual Colonial Secretaries, from Deas-Thom-
son down to Brunker, and 28 distinct Ministries.
Dr. O’Gorman Hughes, a Sydney native
and pupil of the Riverview Jesuits, has been ap-
pointed to a resident medical post at Prince
Alfred Hospital, Sydney, and another similar
appointment is in view. Thus hard words about
cliqueism will happily soon be things of the past.

General, now says he will not attend Court cere-

Ex-police-magistrate T. A. Browne (“Rolf
Boldrewood ”) is now to reside at Melbourne,
where he will be connected with the Argus and
Australasian. He hopes also to familiarise him-
self with the practical work of staging dramas,
with a view to cutting up some of his own novels
for such presentation.
The Argus mentions Tallerman’s death,
in London, about six weeks ago. Having great
business interests in Melbourne, he sensibly made
himself a kind of Agent-General, and, though
riches took wings, as they will do with men of
such aspirations, he always ranked with West-
garth, as a not altogether selfish absentee.
When Acting-Governor Madden courted
Frank Stephen’s daughter it was a bit of an
aspiration, and he won Frank’s heart by making a
spring cart, a line in which Frank was a dabster.
He made a buggy out of a piano-box, and went to
the goldfields with it, but his steam launch,
worked by a coffee-mill, was most unlucky on the
Gippslancl Lakes.
A photo, of Sir Buxton, S.A. Governor-
designate, reached Adelaide last week. The face
is typical of the evangelical brewer, and the smug,
contented smile proclaims the subject’s satisfac-
tion at the brewery dividends sent by the Lord
during the year. The upper-lip is clean-shaven,
and, except for the beard, Buxton is almost a
replica of pastor Jefferis. Why does the evan-
gelical saint always shave and la Brigham Young?
Cabled demise : Yesey Fitzgerald. Over 40
years ago he was regarded, by the Colonial Office,
as the nicely-squared-off, appropriate official to
be Vic. Chief Secretary, and accordingly they
sent him to collaborate with Gov. Hotham. He
was then called Foster, but dropped it after failing
so dismally. He showed up in Melb. again, an
insignificant little man.
The determination of N.S.W. Lieut.-
Governor Darley to hold the Queen’s Birthday
levee on May 27, Parkes’ birthday, recalls John
Haynes’ pleasantry of several years ago, to the
effect that Parkes would have been born on May
24 only that he wished the national celebrations of
his anniversary not to clash with those of Queen
Victoria. And now they will clash.
Alex. Stewart, long noted as the oldest
living resident of Illawarra (N.S.W.) district,
died last week, aged 85. He was born in Ireland
in 1809, and arrived at Wollongong on the 24th
Feb., 1828, when there were not more than 50
males, 10 married women, and 16 children in the
whole of the coastal district from Belli to Jam-
beroo. He served for some years in the N.S.W.
police force, and was a shining light of teetotalism.
Dear Bulletin, —I found yet another
legal Stephen while recently exploring the old v
Melb. cemetery, in which Batman is buried. The
inscription runs thus : “In memory of Henry
George Alfred Stephen, third son of Mr. Justice
Stephen, New Zealand, who closed a life of
amiability and promise Jan. 10, A.D., 1851, at
the early age of 21 years.” The shadow of Bat-
man’s obelisk falls across this tomb. Batman was
born in Parramatta, N.S.W., in the year 1800.—
Yours, &c., C.R.W.
In a Melb. court a Chow washerman, the
other day, sued the son of a great novelist for
arrears of shirt-cleansing. As several months had
elapsed between the time the debt was contracted
and the date of taking out the summons, the Bench
inquired the reason. John explained that his for-
bearance was due to the “ lespeck ” he had for the
creditor’s great father. An original tribute to
genius. Which recalls, by the way, that in Sydney
a memorial to old Jack Robertson is now being
promoted by Mr. Quong Mao Tart, of China.
Poor old Jimmy Munro carries a farewell
letter from Lord Hopetoun next to his ancient
Caledonian heart. One of Hopetoun’s last acts of
state was the posting of a complimentary yarn to
each of the five Premiers who had flourished
during his term of office. Jimmy was one of the
illustrious quintette. It seems ages ago since
Victoria’s destiny was ruled by that abnormal
banker, yet he really did rule up to the time
when the health of his financial concerns sug-
gested a hasty trip to London. He was one of
the meteors that flashed across Gov. Hopetoun’s
regime. Therefore, he got a vice-regal document,
thanking him for having been such a credit to the
country, and loyal supporter of the Queen, and so
nice a man to talk to in the Executive Council
chamber, Jimmy is aggressively proud of this
talisman. He thrusts it under the noses of chance
acquaintances, requesting them to read that. A
letter of praise and thanks from His Excellency is
a complete answer to the cry of the calumniator,
in J. Munro’s opinion. If he were to suddenly
meet the irate depositor who once plugged him in
Collins-st., the ex-Premier would whip his certi-
ficate of virtue from his breast-pocket, and scream
“ Look at that !”
The next Academy work of young Ber-
tram Mackennal, the Australian sculptor, will,
The Bulletin hears from London, enhance the
artist’s already high reputation. The subject,
from the Book of Proverbs, is entitled “The
Frivolous Woman,” and represents the woman
seated with her feet resting upon a figure of Love,
gazing disdainfully at supposed passers-by.
Mackennal had to be very wary in his treatment
of the subject after his experience of the goody-
good i ness of the Academy people in connection
with his Circe last year.
Cabled that W. A. Horn, ex-Gov. Kin-
tore’s friend, is to be knighted for his “services to
Australian exploration.” Bosh ! The ex-S.A.
squatter and stockbroker guided Kintore’s Broken
Hill specs., by which the latter cleared about
.£25,000, and Kintore is now to be on the London
directorate of Wilson’s big Westralian co., in
which Horn holds a large interest. The coming
knighthood is not a reward for public services.
Horn is a wealthy man, and a Tory who was
known to a limited circle in S. A.; and he never did
anything which will be remembered.
Premier Turner is ubiquitous. Although
up late every night over tariff reform and retrench-
ment and referendum problems, he still finds time
to preside at meetings of tin-pot suburban football
clubs, &o. One recent evening was devoted to
distributing the season’s prizes of an obscure
cricket-club. The Premier apparently views such
affairs with much gravity, and when “ distri-
buting ” a prize to Johnnie Jones, as the season’s
champion alley-player, he looks as if it were the
most important moment in the history of the
cosmos.
Here is the first bit of wattle-blossom
“ waving ” over the bushman’s grave :—
It is from the earliest Tasmanian magazine, and
its author, as he deserved, was hanged.
As so few of the old Governors of N.S. W.
have left direct descendants, it is specially interest-
ing to note that on 24th April, at St. Paul’s, Bur-
wood, Sydney, G. B. D. King, of Goonoo Goonoo,
Tamworth (N.S.W.), was married to Miss Amelie
Lloyd, daughter of Mr. C. W. Lloyd, ofLlanarth,
Burwood. The bridegroom is fifth in descent
from Governor King, who ruled in “Botany Bay”
in 1800. At the wedding were present representa-
tives of many old Australian families : The Kings,
Coxs, Campbells, MacArthurs, &c.—not the least
conspicuous group being that comprising Mr. P. G.
King, M.L.O., and Mrs. King, surrounded by
their sons, grandsons and a great grand-daughter.
George Ranken, who died the other day
at Young, N.S.VV., aged 68, was a pioneer of
something more than local celebrity. He accom-
panied the explorer Landsborough through Cen-
tral Queensland in the early fifties, and took up
station property there, afterwards holding various
responsible positions under Government. On
coming to N.S.W., he was appointed a member of
two Commissions to report on the land laws, on
which subject he was a high authority. He
had considerable literary talent, and was a valued
contributor to this journal in its early days. A
volume of his “Bush Essays” was published in
Edinburgh in 72, and his novel “ Windabyne ” is
shortly to be issued in London. He also com-
piled a useful geography of Australia. George
Ranken was emphatically a “ white man ”—large-
hearted, generous, and enthusiastic —and grey
hairs never altered the beat of the heart always
young in his bosom.
THE BARMAPS[?] (FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I’M a simple little
barmaid, but there
ain’t no flies on
me;
I’m as chipper as
they make ’em, so
are all my family.
My purse is never
empty, and my jewels
can command
A fair amount of ooftish
from each “ uncle ”in
the land.
My complexion’s due to
Beddome, and my
gamboge hair to
Brown,
And I wear the very
smartest frocks and
swellest hats in town.
You ask me how I do
it? How I go the
pace ding-dong?
Well, I only laugh and
sing again the bar-
maid’s little song—
Are you coming
in to-night ?
Are you coming
in to-night ?
That’s the tune to mash
the johnnies, and it
really knocks ’em
quite !
Are you coming
in to-night ?
Are you coming
in to-night ?
That’s the carol of the
simple little barmaid
I’m a simple little
barmaid, but I really
know the ropes ;
I know just how to
kindle and extinguish
little hopes ;
I know exactly what’s
required to work a
Sunday drive
As far as Mordialloc,
with a champagne
tea at five;
I know precisely what
to do to get a Prin-
cess box,
With an oyster supper
after, and the best of
German hocks;
I can work a trip to
“Tassie,”too, though
some folks say that’s
wrong;
But I only laugh and
sing again the bar-
maid’s little song—
Are you coming
in to-night ?
Are you coming in to-night?
There’s a world of meaning in it, if you only
read it right!
Are you coming in to-night ?
Are you coming in to-night?
That’s the carol of the simple little barmaid !
I go in strong for racing, yet a bet I never lose;
I smile upon a bookie, and the tip he can’t
refuse;
Then twenty pounds to nothing is the entry
that he makes,
And I really can’t fall in, you know, because
I risk no stakes.
Melb.
And when I fade, and boys don’t hover rom.d
as once they did,
I’ll wed some rich, young squatter who makes
for my heart a bid,
And later figure in Toorak as Lady Billabong ;
Yes, oft the pathway to Toorak is the bar-
maid’s little song—
Are you coming in to-night?
Are you coming in to-night ?
I’ll use the same old song to make some silly
squatter bite!
Are you coming in to-night ?
Are you coming in to-night?
That’s the carol of the simple little barmaid !
STEBLE GREY.
Beneath this sod the bush man lies,
Mingled with mother earth ;
While onward rolls in buoyant course
The world in wanton mirth.
Mimosas green bedeck the spot,
Their branches mournful wave.
Murmuring a dirge, in th’ evening wind.
Over the bushman’s grave.

POLITICAL POINTS
Victorian civil-servants are now only
allowed tires for three instead of five months in
the year. Retrenchment!
The Q. junior bar yearns for a new Attor
n ey-General. The Crown now looks after its
friends, and its own friends only.
Melb. Age, on Parkes : “We can never
forget that to his diseased vanity and obstruction
we owe it that Australian Federation has been
indefinitely delayed.”
The Turner Government purposes wiping
out the vicious Vic. Rolls Purification Act at the
earliest opportunity. To the political “ purists ”
all democratic votes were impure,
Westralian squatter-made law : For steal-
ing a fat pig, value £5, a man can get off with a
fine of £2 and costs; the penalty for thieving a
scrub-fed sheep, worth 55., is seven years !
No man will be acceptable as a N.S.W.
Civil - service commissioner whose public and
private character does not stand as high as that of
the average occupant of a seat on the Supreme
Court Bench,
Prisoners employed in the cookhouse at
Pentridge (Vie.) prison distil from a root, grown
about the quarries, a drink which is beastly to
faat-P But it makes them drunk—and sick next
day. Is Prohibition possible?
All political agitation in Australia should
have one aim—the smashing of the Legislative
Councils and the initiation ot the Referendum.
Until that is done, the people will only get what
the Councils choose to concede.
Gov. Buxton, the new beer-viceroy of S.A.,
is a pious man with a holy horror of gambling in
every shape. It is therefore gratifying to the
unregenerate to know that his salary will be paid
in part out of the license-fees from the sinful tote.
7J Harper, the rampant Vic. Freetrade M. L. A.,
o nnplains bitterly of the proposal to reduce by one-
half the 20 per cent, duty on linseed oil, which,
he says, in a letter to the press, “ is by no means
excessive”—and solemnly urges the retention of
the old impost. The M.P., it seems, was just
ab >ut starting a linseed-oil mill, and as a con-
sistent Freetrader he wants Protection for his own
littleindustry. It is quite right he should be pro-
tected, but the spectacle of the Freetrade M.P.
stating that a 20 per cent, duty on his own mill
“is by no means excessive” is grotesque.
The new duties put on without notice by
Freetrade Premier Reid have already, according
to the commercial editor of the Sydney Tele-
graph, raised the price of sago and tapioca 90 per
cent.; rock salt, 60 per cent.; raw coffee, 25 per
cent.; and linseed oil, 20 to 25 per cent. As N.S.
Wales isn’t, so far as at present known, a country
where coffee or sago or tapioca can be profitably
grown, these duties won’t create any industry or
supply any employment—they merely raise prices.
The Freetrader doesn’t mind raising prices—all he
is afraid of is lest he may be betrayed into helping
some Australian industry ; so the “Dibbs duties,”
which furnished revenue and supplied work at
the same time, come off, and the Reid
duties, which furnish revenue, but are
guaranteed not to supply any work, go
on in their stead.

commission.
lb is publicly rumored that several mem-
bers of both Vic. Houses have, by compounding
with their cronlVors, forfeited their seats. Who
will “bell the cat?” Heavy penalties are men-
tioned in the (Constitution Act, in which the
successful presenter would share—assuming that
there is any salvage. A curious difficulty occurs
in the case of (malpositions. A member who goes
insolvent loses Kis seat, but on being whitewashed
may again contest it. But no such provision is
made in the case of a “compounder.” So that
the inference is that he is not eligible for election
until he has paid 20s. in the £-probably with
interest added.
Queenslanders cannot be too often remind-
ed, in face of the present Ministerial jubilations
over increased revenue, that <£ 1,400,000 has been
added, or is about to bo added, to the debt of the
province by conversion of revenue-deficits into
funded debt.
It is asserted that Premier Kingston stood
alone against his five colleagues in opposing rail-
way-chairman Smith’s re-appointment. He re-
mained obstinate, and, after a delay of over six
weeks, Pendleton was appointed as a compromise.
Smith may possibly be offered the Yic. commis-
sionership, which will be open in Oct.
Yic. income-tax is responsible for some
queer attempts at evasion. A Brunswick pro-
perty-owner, who had six houses unoccupied for
18 months, becoming frightened at the prospect
of further taxation, solved the difficulty by pulling
the tenements to pieces and selling the timber,
brick, &o. But later on ho found that the pro-
ceeds were regarded as income and came within
the operations of the Act.
Major-General Hutton will receive, as per
agreement, .£320 from N.S.W. Treasury to pay
his travelling expenses back to England early next
year. The expenses are described as including
his own, his wife’s, and a servant’s. This sum,
on a rough estimate, approaches the Duncan
Gillies scale, and while Governments exhibit such
liberality their frenzied cries for departmental
retrenchment are mere melodrama.
Dear Bulletin, —The subsidising of the
Sydney unemployed is a new form of Protection.
They are given tucker, a free railway-pass to the
goldfields of the province, and a miner’s right.
Now, the country man, equally poor, who has no
bureau to go to or means to reach the Sydney one,
and who mines without a right, if found out, is
heavily fined. Where is the justice in the dis-
tinction ?— A. F. Spring. Young, May 12, ’95.
Vic. Govt, replies to Judge Moles worth re
docking half his salary while on three weeks’ sick
leave, that orders have been issued to apply the
same rule to all, from the lowest to the highest.
The Judge had taken it that he was suspected of
“malingering.” But the rule is a good one. The
Bulletin learns that Solicitor-General Outhbert
is now sick and about to take a health-trip co
S.A. It will be interesting to learn that he
has applied the same undiscriminating rule to
himself (“the highest ”).
H. : “I must join issue with The Bul-
letin about the Judges. I contend they ought
all to be under the Government, which is respon-
sible to the people. Robespierre’s principle, in
this matter, is the safest; that, in practice, we
ought never to assume anybody to be more
virtuous than the bulk of the people, and if these
be otherwise, why, the community falls to pieces.
On the other assumption, that the people are bad
and the few good have to be fenced in with
privileges, you open the door for the worst abuses
that characterise the English nation. All the
magistracy must thus be roped in, as well as the
Judges, who are only higher magistrates. The
American Judges are just (about as good as the
English.”

(Authentic.)
Instead of a Supreme Court Judge being
entitled to a full pension for life (of, in N.S. W.,
£lBOO a year) if permanently disabled by illness
ten minutes after he gets his appointment, it
would be advisable to grant a pension of (say)
one-fourth of the regulation allowance to the dig-
nitary who collapses any time within the first five
years of his service ; of one half to the one who
falls in harness at any time between his fifth and
tenth year ; and of two-thirds to the man who
outlasts his tenth year but does not complete his
fifteen years’ term, while only the man who does
his full term will be entitled to the whole amount.
Then the eminent barrister with a diseased lung
or deranged liver, who knows that he will pro-
bably break down after a very few years’ service,
would have less inducement than at present to
accept judicial office, and would cost less if he did
accept it, and the country would get better value
for its money all round.

A sporting member of N.S.W. Assembly :
“Poor old Parkes’ speech was a pitiable affair,
but Premier Reid’s reply put me on pins and
needles. He had all the cards in his hands
and up his sleeve, and was very brave; he kept
on battering at the old chap, who sat there shiver-
ing with cold—in fact, looking more dead than
alive. I don’t say that old man Parkes didn t
deserve most of what he got, and he did catch it,
with a vengeance ; but if I’d been Varney Parkes
I couldn’t have helped jumping over the back
bench and plugging Georgey-Porgey m the eye
when he called my old father * a tom-cat.
The proposed settlement of the railway-
war between New South Wales, Victoria and
S. A. for the Riverina traffic has fallen through,
and the old struggle will still go on. It will pro-
bably keep going on till a Federal Parliament
wipes out the venerable quarrel, along with a lot
of others. The compromise failed, as usual,
because each province “wanted the earth,” and
the one which thought it was going to get a little
less of it than the others backed out. Everything
went well until it cn-me to the rush for the odd
half-penny, and then all semblance of unity
vanished.
Cabled that a Bill is to be introduced into
the Lords authorising the appointment of
“colonial” Judges to the Privy Council. This
remarkable eagerness to rush the matter through
is another reason why the Australian Parliaments
should go slow in the matter. The last time the
British Government was in a hurry about a mere
“ colonial” matter was when the rascally Naval
Tribute scheme was scurried through. And every
other time when it has bustled itself about the
affairs of Greater Britain, it has been over some-
thing concerning which “ the colonies ” were more
or less sold, or were intended to be.
This extract from an English judgment on
an employers’-liability case shows that even in
that land of “ freedom ” the glorious principle of
“ freedom of contract ” is gradually growing
“cronk.” Soon, no one but the ARGUS will
maintain the “gag” :
It can’t be said, where a man is lawfully engaged in
work and is in danger of dismissal if he leaves his work,
that he wilfully incurs any risk which he may encounter
in the course of such work. If the plaintiff could have
o-one away from the dangerous place without incurring
the lisk of losing his means of livelihood, the case might
have been different, but he was obliged to be there: his
poverty, not Ids will, consented to incur the danger.
Vide Eagleson on employers’ liability, p. 181.
The last sentence is worth remembering in “free-
dom of contract ” discussions.
There are some extraordinarily small
constituencies in Westralia, resembling the
“rotten boroughs” before the Reform Bill
of ’32. In the W.A. Legislative Assembly F.
Connor represents East Kimberley (26 electors !).
Murchison boasts 37 electors, whilst R. F. Sholl,
member for Gascoyne, a huge area of country,
only represents 24 electors. Thirty-seven pastor-
alists constitute De Grey electorate, whilst
haughty Attorney-General Burt (member for
Ashburton) is sent to Parliament by 52 people.
Alick Forrest (the Premier’s brother) represents
98 in West Kimberley, and Horace W. Sholl is
sent to the House by 91 for Roebourne. Even
the Premier, who has got a family seat in Bun-
bury, is only the mouthpiece for 262 persons.
The Victorian income-tax is now estimated
to yield only about £155,000 or £160,000 a year,
and £50,000 of the amount is alleged to be contri-
buted by civil servants. This circumstance has
roused a number of shallow persons to a frenzied
belief that one-third of the aggregate income of
the country is that which the State pays to a set
of bloated bureaucrats. The much more reason-
able explanation is that the civil servant must
give his official income correctly, because his state-
meats are checked by the Estimates in other
words, that he is a compulsory G. Washington ;
and that the rest of the community has lied dis-
gracefully and horribly. There was such a dire
necessity for money that no time could be spent in
verifying assertions, no matter how glaring ; the
schedules were rushed through at the rate of 1000
per day, and no questions asked.
The members of the S.A. Parliamentary
Labor-party look nervously forward to the general
elections next April. Yet a little while ago they
talked about capturing all the seats and dis-
lodging Kingston, Solierk, Jenkins, George xiaw-
ker, and other members who, though they invari-
ably vote on the Liberal side, are not Trades Hall
nominees.

“cussing’’ and dis-

“ What wonder that such laws should
become hateful, and that standing here on the
spot where I first drew breath—in sight of a
levelled home, with memories of privations and
tortures crowding on my mind, I should swear to
devote the remainder of my life to the destruction
of what has blasted my early years, pursued me
with its vengeance through manhood, and leaves
my family in exile to-day far from that Ireland
which is itself wronged, robbed, and humiliated
through the agency of the same accursed system !
It is no little consolation to know, however, that
I may yet have the satisfaction of trampling on
the ruins of Irish landlordism.'’ — Michael Davitt,
at a meeting in County Mayo, Feb. 1880.
If “revenue duties” were imposed (says
the Argus) Civil Service retrenchment would be
superfluous. The germ of a great idea :
The object of this par. is to state, in answer
to inquiries, that Mademoiselle Emilie Estevenet
is the same costumiere who was at Messrs. Alston
and Brown’s establishment a few years ago. When
that firm went out of business, Mdlle. Emilie
returned to Paris, and subsequently to London;
and, in both centres of fashion, gained a leading
reputation for distinctiveness and originality, in
addition to the other attributes of a court dress-
maker. Among other royalties, she designed
gowns for H.I.H. the German Empress. Mdlle.
Emilie has now engaged her services to Messrs.
Hicks, Atkinson and Sons, of Collins-st., Melb.
If our goods were all imported, if we made none
of our own,
And if duties were for revenue and that alone
collected,
There would be no vile retrenchment and no
cutting to the bone—
We might all have higher wages than we’ve
hitherto expected.
Calculate the many millions that are squandered
on the hands
Making hats and boots and other things too
numerous to mention,
Which, if brought from German factories or from
other foreign lands,
Would return a mighty revenue and abolish all
dissension.
That Protection’s howling madness is as plain as
A.8.C.,
Try the freer-trader’s principle, which full of
homely nous is,
Then might all be civil-servants and as happy as
can be
On the money gathered daily in the busy
Custom Houses.

AN IMPORTANT OPINION.

Although the N.S. W. public have suc-
ceeded in getting a Royal Commission in the Dean
poisoning-case, yet they have hardly got what they
wanted. Instead of a straightforward, impartial
enquiry into the question of Dean’s innocence,
they are treated to the spectacle of the law officers
of the Crown defending themselves vigorously
against a charge of mal-administration.
• • •
“Is it not a fact that habitual arsenic-
, eaters can take what are to others poisonous doses
daily without apparent ill results; until, sud-
denly, at some other weak turn of health, and
especially if they cease the daily dose, they sud-
denly exhibit severe symptoms of arsenical poison-
ing? ” In ’75, at the 48th annual meeting of the
German Society of Naturalists and Physicians,
Dr. Knapp, practising in Styria, introduced two
male arsenic-eaters to the Assembly. One of
these men consumed, in the presence of the
savants, more than 6grs. white arsenic—enough to
poison three ordinary men—without suffering the
slightest inconvenience. Arsenic-eating is a com-
mon practice among the ox-herds and shepherds
of Styria, where it is known by the name of
fiedri. Hedrists, once they commence the habit,
cannot leave it off. If an arsenic-eater discon-
tinues his indulgence, the last dose kills him —the
sudden cessation of the habit being the cause of
death. Thus a Continental toxicologist: “ The
gradual cessation produces such a heart-growing
that it may probably be said that no genuine
arsenic-eater ceased to eat arsenic while life
lasted.” So early as 1822, Dr. Heisoh first discussed
the subject of arsenic-eating, and, in 1851, Tschudi
brought the matter prominently before medical
science. Since that time scientific research has
amply proved Hedri to be no fiction. The Styrians
commence with minute doses, usually taken once
a week ; then twice a week, then once a day ; the
doses being steadily increased in strength. The
habit is hereditary, so that the present race is
almost arsenic-proof. Want of the poison is the
thing that kills, yet, when death ensues, the corpse
has all the symptoms of arsenical poisoning.
« • •
Poisoned rabbits, it is averred, are being
sent to Sydney for sale. It has been represented
that regular consignments of arsenic and strych-
nine-saturated rabbits are received from the runs
and sold for human food. However, in view of
the marvellous toxicological discoveries lately
made by N.S. W. Supreme Court the remedy is
obvious. If after eating rabbit and onions you
find yourself suffering from arsenical poisoning,
all you need do is to load yourself up with
strychnined rabbit. If, on the other hand, a meal
of rabbit produces tetanic convulsions, the obvious
remedy is lOgrs. arsenic every half-hour. Shake
the bottle well.

Melb. Pressman : “ I am a Protestant my-
self, but must confess that the courtesy and cor-
diality (whisky is a cordial) of the Catholic priests,
as compared with the high and haughty—even
insolent —demeanour of the Anglican pilots, are
tending to weaken my faith. As a scribe, I called
on Bishop Goe recently for an advance copy of his
sermon in MS. After being kept waiting on the
Episcopalian doorstep for some time, the flunkey
told me that ‘his lordship’ would allow me to
copy the deliverance if I called at 9 a.m,
on Sunday. I went, after walking three
miles, and was told to sit down in the pala-
tial hall and copy the precious document.
A few Sundays after I had occasion to report
Archbishop Carr, and the difference in treatment
astounded me. When I enquired for the MS. I
was welcomed by the prelate in person, who con-
siderately refreshed me, and said he would ask a
Holy Father to make a copy and hand it to me
after service. Going at the appointed time I
found the MS. copied in a clear, schoolboy hand
—and more, I was asked in by the Archbishop
and supped merrily with him and his priestly
following, not a word being said about religion or
the object of my mission. This, mark you, is a
typical occurrence. The priest is a man of the
world, and doesn’t pretend to be a bit better than
he is. It is not the fault of Australian heretic
pressmen if the R.C. faith isn’t boomed whenever
occasion presents itself.”
• * *
Illustrative of the system of allowing Judges’
relatives to practise before “ the old man ” :
During a more or less recent circuit. Judge X. pre-
sided over a case in which his young and recently-
“ called ” relative, Mr. X., held a brief for defen-
dant. Mr. X. has not yet gained that experience
which will probably come to him hereafter. And
so, when plaintiff’s counsel asked a question
which was clearly inadmissible, no one was very
greatly surprised that Mr. X. should allow it to
pass. The Judge, however, shifted uneasily and
looked hard at his kinsman—who, in happy un-
consciousness, continued to idly pull a quill_ to
pieces, and failed to notice his Honor’s meaning
glances. So, in sheer desperation, the Judge
took the matter into his own hands.
“Surely, Mr. ,” he said, with affected sur-
prise—“surely you do not think of asking that
question!” “Certainly,” was the reply ; “it is
not objected to by the other side.” “Well, you
needn’t be in such a hurry. You might give Mr.
X. a chance to open his mouth.” Even a man
engrossed in pulling a quill to pieces could not fail
to take this hint, and Mr. X. at last rose to his
feet and did object.

Melbourne is simultaneously asked to do
a week of self-denial for its chief hospital and to
roll up to a theatrical carnival (run by the Gaiety
Girlhood) in aid of the Old Actors’ Home. Which
charity will Melb. choose, think you ?
This year, Sydney, Melbourne and Ade-
laide respectively will be treated to the tawdry
spectacle of a vice-vice-regal levee in honor of their
Gracious Queen’s birthday. A Governor’s levee
is a vain thing. A rally of smirking citizens for
the purpose of shaking hands with the bedizened
shadow of a shade!

A Melb. girl is bewailing her faithless
lover an officer on one of the Austrian men-o’-
war which visited these waters some two years
ago. During his absence the lieutenant has
written and sent gifts of barbaric gems and gold
to the maiden from time to time. Now lie writes
that he is medically advised not to marry, as
“something has gone wrong with his heart.”

Rev. Stone-Wigg, of Brisbane, analysing
offertories for nine Sundays from Septuagesima to
Palm Sunday,says that, taking average attendance,
morning and evening, at 800, only 390 gave any-
thing, and 60 per cent, gave threepence or under.
“This,” says the indignant parson, “is simply
shameful.” Why? Isn’t salvation free in Bris-
bane ?

Apropos of the cabled arrest in Jersey of
an allegedly-defaulting Maoriland attorney, a
very interesting return could be compiled of noto-
rious offenders who have eluded justice, and either
have never been again heard of or are safely
established where detectives cease from troubling
and extradition is at rest. The Vie. police heard
some time ago that a bank official who absconded
during Easter with Jl7OOO is located in the Trans-
vaal, where he is prosperously running a general
store. There is no treaty, and lie can laugh at
the law.

At a fire in Melbourne, a few
days ago, two adjoining shops were
burnt down. The one Was a patent-
corset maker’s, and the other was
stocked with Catholic and Pagan
images. The Devil might have looked
after his own—though whether the
corsets or the images, it is not for
The Bulletin to say.
# # #
Two aboriginal girls, aged 15
and 13, were baptised at Toowoomba
(Q.), the other day, in the course of a
service which lasted six hours, and
was graced by the presence of three
Ms.L. A. (organising for the election
in prospect). The girls were specially
imported from Mackay, 400 miles
North. Q. philosophers have already
remarked how diligently the guileless
missionaries labor for the salvation of
young gins.

There is much difference in the dietary-
scales of some of the Australian prisons. For
instance, Darlinghursb is much preferred, by the
roving criminal, to PeiHridge. In the big yic.
prison there is an unchanging menu of boiled
mutton and beef with vegetables. Pudding is
allowed only once a year—at Xmas, to inspire the
confinees with gentle feelings. At Darlinghurst,
on the other hand, roast dishes, corned beef, and
stews, with suet dumplings and treacle pudding,
make practicable a palatable daily variation. Men
who have done the rounds of all the provincial
gaols declare that Victorian prisoners are th e
worst-fed of the lot. The only redeeming featur^

Bathurst, whose gaol has lately been the
scene of a number of rebellious uprisings, cul-
minating in homicide in O’Connor’s case, and in
attacks on warders in various fashions, with
floggings and ironings as consequences, is regain-
ing some of its ancient prison reputation. The
fiercest of N.S.W. prison revolts occurred there
nearly 70 years ago. In the conflict two men and
five horses were killed, and when the outbreak was
quelled hundreds of floggings were administered
and ton mon wore hnmrod
and ten men were hanged.

The Anonci nnbliohes a fist, of so colled
ine ARGUS puDUSties a list or so-called
mistakes made by Melb. Sunday scholars, answer-
ing Biblical questions. Most of them seem, to a
dispassionate reader, evidences of common-sense,
with not a few smart pieces of sarcasm. There is
a national bias in the juvenile mind towards
Truth and against Terewth which is very hard to
kill utterly.
A now prosperous Sydney soli-
citor, who had a hard row to hoe when
he started practice (his first six months
yielded exactly .£8 10s.), tells the story
of his first client. He defended the man
on a criminal charge at the instance of a
friend who had, out of charity, been
supportingthe accused’s wifeand family
whilst the bread-winner was in gaol
awaiting trial. Our attorney aided in
securing prisoner’s acquittal, “ and you
would have thought,” he says, “that
the whole boiling of ’em—man, wife and
children—would have expired out of
sheer gratitude to me. I certainly never
expected them to recover. As they
hadn’t a red cent, I told the man that I
didn’t want to make any money out of
the thing, butthat I was poor myself
and would like to get back the £9 odd I
had paid out of pocket for counsel, etc.
They swore to repay within six months,
but I never heard a word from them for
quite a year, and had almost determined
to forget about the matter when one
night I saw them in the theatre-stalls,
all well-dressed and enjoying them-
selves hilariously. This aroused my gall
somewhat, and I wrote the ex-prisoner
a letter saying that, as he now seemed
fairly prosperous, he might possibly be
able to pay me my £9. In response,
his wife came to see me. Quoth she :
“Of course, we’ll pay you when we
make some money, bub really I don’t
think you ought to worry about it. I’m
quite sure you did it for an advertise-
ment.”
There is a mighty moral in this. No-
body who does charity publicly gets (or
deserves) credit nowadays for being
actuated by anything more lofty than
Advt. Thie only people really charit-
able at heart are those who do good
works—and studiously keep their names
out of the newspapers.
Dear Bulletin, —Be article on pick-
pockets. I happen to know the boss man, an
Australian-born. He is the dazzler of the profes-
sion, and made his friends’ hair stand on end by
the way he cleaned out a lot of the parsons
assembled at Spencer-street, Melbourne, to wel-
come Talmage. Also, he was the leading genius
in the famous Ballarat Bank robbery, when a
large safe was taken away and left in a marsh,
but he had real bad luck in another city, where a
detective caught him in the act of cleaning out a
provincial. For this he did three years. A little
square-built, nerveful man, with keen, penetrating
eyes, an ideal bank-teller. He has distinctly
raised the tone of the profession, and all Austra-
lians should be proud of him.—E. M. Watts,
Adelaide.

boom-built suburb, the wretched paupers of in-
habitants go to a pork-factory and. get three-

to give you your dinner. One poor devil of a
mother (from England, of course) says it causes
“ trinchinopoly ”in the children, bub they’ve gob
bo live somehow, trinchinopoly or no trinohino-
poly.

Gall from retiring country editor :
Several readers have complained that the Express is
too much alive—matter is written too brightly—and that
they prefer their literature dished up in the style usually
characteristic of the country press. In order to appease
those readers, and to show that the Express can write
pars, of the proper sort, we beg to state that our worthy
citizen, Mr. James Smith, the local cahdriver, is suffering
from a bad attack of influenza. He is in good medical
hands, and his fellow citizens hope ere long to S( e him
well and handling the ribbons in his usual accomplished
style.

ST. ALBAN’S ESTATE (Geelong). Avenue in Garden looking West.
Photo, by Talma.
An enterprising “beat” is working the
southern suburbs of Melbourne on “a new lay.”
He carries a tattered Bible with him, and deals
with parsons, church elders, and pious females.
He exhibits his fragmentary Bible to sympathetic
souls, and begs for a good one, with gentle
unction and copious quotations of apropos texts.
Be can do with little bread and poor raiment, he
says, with an air of superhuman resignation, but
he pines without the Word. Result, in nine cases
out of ten, a new or second-hand copy of the Real
Secret of England’s greatness, and a shilling or
so for creature comforts. A grand clearing-sale
of new and second-hand Bibles pending.
Photo, by King.
The Late GEORGE RAN KEN.

TOO BAD.
Three lady bicyclists they were,
New Women, garbed in knickers gay,
Who posed without a tremor there,
Admired by then who passed that way,
Till one came up, a man of phlegm,
Who eyed them o’er, and loudly said ;
“My oath ! there ain’t no flies on them.”
And then they blushed and turned and fled.
Melb.

Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will he taken of communications insuffi*
ciently stamped. Name and address must
appear on MS. No liability is undertaken re
contributions voluntarily submitted, and in no
case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of
any province) accompany.

In addition to being the finest country
property in Victoria, the St. Alban’s estate (Gee-
long) is one of the finest and most complete racing
establishments in the world. It is absolutely
unrivalled in Australia, and if there is a better
place in Europe no travelling Australian has yet
found it. The copious water-supply, the large
paddocks of English grasses, and the spick-and-
span completeness of the entire equine equipage
are at once the delight and the envy of every
breeder of horses who visits Mr. W. R. Wilson’s
famous property. Shelter sheds of large capacity
are placed in many of the paddocks, while, the year-
ling quarters consist of 40 loose boxes, cool and well
ventilated, built inquadrangularform, andof ample
size. Stone and brick tanks, windmills and feed-
bins are placed in convenient places in the various
paddocks, and the yards, &0., are amply and per-
fectly arranged. In the centre of the estate the
training-track and course proper are laid out (1|
miles and 1 mile 5 furlongs round respectively).
Both may well be compared to Flemington or
Rand wick, the going being good in all weathers.
The scraping-sheds are suitably placed, and an
elevated stand commands a perfect view of the
tracks, river, and the estate generally, with the
far-famed Barrabool Hills in the distance. The
stallion-boxes are lofty and well designed, opening
into a large covered-in exercise ground. Eeed
rooms, men’s rooms, &c., are under the same roof.
The training-stables, &c., contain 15 loose boxes,
feed rooms, men’s rooms, and every necessary
adjunct to a first-class establishment. Tha
out-buildings are extensive and ample. As
for the stock, they are worthy of the estab-
lishment. The sires include the celebrated Tren-
ton, Eiridspord (imported), Robinson Crusoe,
Strathmore and Bill of Portland (imported), and
a very long string of brood mares of distinguished
appearance and ancient lineage,While among the
list of racehorses appear such names as Carnage,
Havoc, Wallace, Challenger, Pilatus, Argent,
Steadfast, Bradford, Trentham, Nada, Warfare,
Conflict, Dillon and many others, all of splendid
appearance and the bluest of patrician blood. Both
the St. Alban’s estate and stud are to be distributed
by “Tattersall ” (Mr. George Adams) on the ait-
union principle at an early date, and a formal
announcement of this event is published else-
where. In this issue appear also a couple of
pictures taken in the St. Alban’s garden which
will serve to illustrate how far taste and wealth
can be employed in developing natural beauties.
Shareholders in the distribution will doubtless be
interested in knowing that the shares are being
taken up very rapidly, and the drawing will be an
event of the near future.

May 21, 1895,
My Dear Moorabxnda—
Mr. Frederick Yilliers, the Most-Travelled
Smythe’s latest exhibit—who was entertained by
a number of representative pressmen at a pleasant
pionic at the Hawkesbury last Sunday—cub a very
taking figure at Sydney Centenary Hall the other
Wednesday, when a huge and three-parts-mascu-
line audience came forth to have anew light cast
upon Celestial suffering, and to revel, as it were,
in purple patches of gore. It almost looks as if
masculine as well as feminine dress-reform were a
howling necessity when a fine, upstanding male,
beautifully dressed in a loose grey suit with fairly
full knickers, coarse, warm stockings, and lace-up
boots, has to apologise to his hearers for not wear-
ing a boiled shirt with a ruby solitaire and a
willie-wagtail like a waiter. People who can dis-
play breezy, unskewered outlines should always
be welcome as flowers in May, no matter how the
fashions go.
The war-correspondent is the image of his Bul-
letin portrait—fierce, out-curving moustache and
all, and the only thing I didn’t like about his tout
ensemble on the Toreador-draped platform was the
communication - wire which you occasionally
detected running from the great man to the o.p.
side. He never seemed to use it—only hung on to
it in a way which rather gave him the appearance
of being on a string. As for his talk—he has the
English accent rather badly, and if many more of
these weary-voiced youths come to our shores to
talk in a worn-out way, the old saying about Aus-
tralians being born tired will need reversal. And
I would also like to warn Mr. Yilliers, and any
other itinerant talker who happens to be listening,
that no man who wants to be respected in Aus-
tralia should ever tell funny stories about how a
blind Chinese mule sent him to grass in one
flourish.
I liked Mr. Yilliers’ discourse, which Australia
has the privilege of sampling before England
does. As the lecturer omits all technicalities, and
never explains stratagems and tactics in camp-
language, his talk is delightfully explicit, from a
woman’s point of view. The discourse, and most
of the realistic photos, taken on the spot, are very
gory; it is in the main real tragedy one is listen-
ing to and looking at, but perhaps the show
would have been a failure otherwise. The photos,
of Jap. officers evoked feminine admiration.
They certainly looked neat in their German
uniforms —but the figure I was specially attracted
to was one of a group of Chinese stewards, who
had on the most perfectly-cut leg-of-mutton sleeve
I have ever seen in my life.
Quite a number of balls are billed to happen
before one has time to turn round three times.
The third annual dance, in aid of the R.O. Church,
Rand (which was such a success last year),
will be held in Paddington Town Hall on Wed-
nesday, 29th inst. Mrs. Humphrey Oxenham
(Mr. Oxenham bought the late Gov. Duff’s
carriage- horses, by the'way) and Mrs. T. Payten
are hon. secs., and tickets are five shillings.
Another five-shilling dance will be held at Need’s
rooms in aid of the piano-fund for the Children’s
Diphtheria Hospital, on Friday evening. 14th prox.
It is alleged that children are fond of music, and
that it is therefore necessary for the nurses to
have a piano. Mrs. F. Lamb and Nurse Proctor
are hon. secs. On June 28 the steamship com-
panies hold their annual ball at Paddington Town
Hall. This dance was both novel and nice last
year, and I am told that the committee mean to
surpass even that success. Then the Fresh Air
League ball committee is getting ready for a
triumphant denouement, and the Tempe ball
workers in a good cause even now report a brisk
ticket sale for their always popular dance on 24th
July. Mrs. Prendergast and Mrs. W, Brennan,
hon. secs.
One public hop that will be missed is the Boys’
Brigade ball, which will not, I hear, be held this
season, although there seems more need for it than
ever since the 8.8. is £2OO in debt. But it appears
that this deserving institution, which gave the
always pathetic street arab a chance to grow into
a decent man, is going to be wound up, as the
elder boys and youths have fallen away and left
the institute to be carried on by a crowd of small
and respectable youngsters who would be much
better at home (as the Acting-Governor opined),
and who were incapable of controlling the affairs
of the club. So the Boys’ Brigade is doomed, for
I don’t think the scheme to start it again on a
smaller scale will win many supporters.

new Chancellor (Judge Windeyer) to the arms of

8.15 p.m. There is a very strong general com-
