The human body is a victim to those
dreadful enemies, the microbes, and to prevent
those treacherous foes to approach you there is no
better method than to keep yourself clean and not
allow food to decay in your mouth or the clogging
of the epidermis. To prevent these things, one of
the most leading chemists in Sweden brought out
his world-renowned Amykos, which, without
doubt, is the best toilet requisite with which to
arargle your throat, wash your mouth and face.
Amykos is strongly recommended as a preventa-
tive of sore throat and bad breath.
ladies and undergrads.”—two divisions of society
which don’t appear to me to have much in
common, and certainly don’t deserve to be dumped
together at 2s. 6d. per caput. Such a classifica-
tion will, I am sure, be resented quite as fiercely
by the undergrads, as by the ladies.
The departure of Lady Duff and her daughters
on the first stage of their journey to England, last
Friday afternoon, was a very doleful proceeding.
It was a dismally web and depressing evening, and
no preparations had been made by the railway
authorities to keep the platform clear, as was done
when the Jerseys left. Consequently a very
higgledy-piggledy crowd —including a number of
aggressive women who evidently wanted to be
violently sympathetic with Lady D. and weep
sisterly tears all over her crape—swarmed round
the sleeping-carriage (the vice-regal snow-white
car wasn’t trotted out) and had to be thrust back
by heavy-weight porters to allow of a path as
wide as my sash ribbon, on the extreme edge of
the platform, being kept clear for Lady Duff and
her party. The train weighed in directly the ex-
vioe-regalities were on board, and so mercifully
cub short a very hipped sorb of ceremony.
Lady Duff, looking pale and sad, and keeping
her head bent, was nob over-trimmed with crape,
and her black dress was simply made for the
voyage in coab-and-skirb style ; a small black crape
bonnet for head-gear. She was escorted by Acting-
Gov. Darley, and accompanied by Mrs. Edward
Hatton—in a quiet black dress with some violets
in her hat. Pretty Miss Kate Duff, in black with
a black gem hat and veil, was escorted by Major-
General Hutton, and the other vice-regal girls
made a gloomy group in their mourning frocks. I
suppose that the deep veil of sadness that
hung over Lady Duff’s departure was regarded as
precluding anything like a demonstrative send-off.
Bub, etiquette or not, I think a few warm farewell
cheers wouldn’t have been amiss to bid goodbye
to a woman who has had a great deal of trouble
on her shoulders, and who didn’t beg the question
when a heavy sorrow was sheeted home to her. I
am sure everybody at the station wished her bon-
voyage, and it seemed a pity that somebody with
good lungs didn’t say so. The Sydney public—
generally so hearty—behaved on this occasion like
a set of spooks.
Lady Norman and Miss Norman are Lady
Duff’s fellow-passengers to England in the Vic-
toria, and Bananaland’s Governor accompanied
his wife and daughter round the coast which he,
too, will quit finally in a few months. There was
a goodly passenger-list of notables who had timed
their voyages with a prescient eye to the advan-
tages of travelling in “ the best of society.” For
if Mrs. Longsleever’s ancestors didn’t come over
with the Normans, it is nice for her to be able to
say that she herself went back with them, any-
way. The Northern vice-royalties have always
been unpretentious and popular, too; and Bris-
bane Grhvmentouse society, during their rule, was
a sufficiently happy family.
People are fond of saying that Governor’s wives
are important personages, and so they are while
they may reflect His Excellency the Governor’s
glory, dla sun and moon. But when H.E. is re-
moved from the scene, down falls Her Ex. from
her high official estate —even the supply of banners
and national anthem is cut off short. Everything
is profoundly plain—even the military daren’t
sport all I heir fancy-work—which proves that the
official status for a Governor’s widow is as good
as nil. Let conceited vice-regal women remember
this when they think they really run the country
because they are more fluent than their husbands.
“ Katherine,” the operetta by Signor Lardelli
and Mrs. Margery Browne, was produced and la
cantata at Sydney Town Hall last Thursday even-
ing, with a very flattering amount of success, con-
sidering there were no dialogues, scenery,
or costumes. The hall was very full, and
some parts of the operetta were heard to
great advantage—notably Katherine’s plaintive
and pretty song “ How passing sweet,”
it is to love,” the doctor’s song and the poet’s
song; the duo (Katherine and Archibald), “Dare
I for her favor pray?” Likewise the soprano
aria, “Now rides the moon in all her regal splen-
dor,” and several more, including the interpolated
“Semper Fidelis”—tenor solo, which created quit©
an effect, and as sung by Mr. Leston, was en-
thusiastically encored.
I think that “ Katherine’s ” weakness as a comio
opera is that it isn’t comic. The composer admits
it isn’t, and still he bills it on the book of words
as a comic opera, and when you invite people to
eggs and bacon and give ’em champagne and
oysters it is naturally difficult for them to adapt
themselves to the situation all at once. lam told
(by some perhaps unauthorised person) that
hungry hiatuses are left in the dialogue (burlesque
style) in order to give the gagger a free
line. I haven’t seen any of the dialogue myself,
and I haven’t met anybody who has, but the songs
and choruses are fairly lucid, if not exactly
brilliant—the meaning is all on the surface, and
neither satire, epigram, nor double meaning has
any place therein. Speaking honestly, the music
deserves a better book. The choruses (not even
decently rendered on Friday—you cannot get up
an operetta with four rehearsals) would go with a
splendid swing if they were given half a chance.
Hecate’s music is perhaps the weakest in the
score —it lacks savageness and inclines to see-saw.
Miss Violet Birkenhead, Mrs. (Dr.) Edwards,
Messrs. Frank Adams, George and Harry
Leston, and an unknown baritone first tackled
“Katherine” in public, with Rivers Allpress as
first violin and Lardelli shaking the stick at ’em.
“Dorothy” went delightfully at Sydney Lyceum
on Saturday before a crowded audience—with, as
luck would have it, the handsomest gown in the
house happily placed in the front row. This gown,
worn by a well-known society woman, was of
black silk with a bodice of black dotted in gold
and fancifully embroidered in glistening golden
flowers both back and front. It was not perhaps
the sort of gown a wise woman would wear to the
“Mikado,” but anywhere else it would always
take the odd trick. A gay-looking d.c. pair were
a soldier and lady—he in red and she in red, like
two faithful tomatoes on one spray. Yet really,
when a woman is doomed to escort a gaudy full-
fig lobster—l mean warrior—it is generally safest
to match him unless you want to set the onlookers’
teeth on edge.
There were one or two discrepancies about the
Dorothy dressing. Nellie S. in a sallow skirt and
a very moderate hat trimmed in a middle-class
way with poppies, wasn’t a patch on her old self
in lavender-blue with hoops of velvet and swarms
of field-flowers all about; but Flo. Young, in
tortoise-shell tints, was nice enough. In the
second act Nellie Stewart was arrayed like a copy
in millinery of the Milky Way, all white with
starry sleeves ; and Flo. Young, in white, artis-
tically draped with chiffon, was furthermore
adorned with crowds of little violets. Brownlow
(in mellow voice for the pUce de resistance, “Queen
of My Heart,” which gained a howling recall—
Mr. B.’s voice, by the way, has developed extra-
ordinary staying-power as contrasted with his
previous Sydney performances) cub a great dash as
Harry Sherwood —especially in the pearl-colored
satin suit richly broidered in gold-and-silyer
thread and terminating in silk stockings. Beside
this finely-feathered warbler, tenor Tapley, in a
green-stuff coat moderately embroidered, and a
blue ribbon round his neck, looked more like the
duke’s friend than the duke himself. Mr. Tapley,
I notice, has only one way of suggesting naughti-
ness, which he expressed by wiping his intellectual
nose. A tendency to titivate one’s proboscis with
a giddy lace handkerchief is a sign of great larki-
ness according to the stage gospel of the “ gentle-
manly tenor.”
Contrary to my expectations (I never expect
much from a piece of music which comes to you
tied up with pink ribbon), Miss Annie Summer-
belle’s new song, “Unrest” (words by Goethe,
translated by Longfellow), is distinctly clever and
melodious. The song is for contralto, mezzo-
soprano, or baritone—it has been sung with great
success by Deslouis, and is altogether worthy of
the encomiums passed upon it by that excellent
judge. The grandeur of the words goes without
saying, and the melody is written in the plaintive
key of F very simply and gracefully. Altogether,
Miss Summerbelle has concocted a most dignified
song.
Give us this day our daily waltz. The little list
of original music for this week includes the
“ Boronia,” by E. J. Peterson, gaudily illustrated
in primal colors on the outside, and set to a
humble little tune (but still a tune) with fairly
good rhythm. The “ Harbor ” waltz, by Elsie
Gerrard, dedicated to Lady Hopetoun (why nob
to the more imminent and aquatic Lady Brassey?)
is the work of a lady who might some day—after
much musical wrestling of spirit—turn out some-
thing worth printing; but the “ Harbor” does not
strike me as that something, although the band
did play it at the S.T.C. races. Paling & Cos.
send me the “Naval Waltz,” by Daphne, which
strikes me as being well-named. Anybody who
has struggled with a member of the unterpsichorean
navy will see exactly where the point comes in.
The “Norman Waltz” —dedicated to Gov. Nor-
man by Lily Macalister, who florally interprets
her front-name on the cover —is the kind of com-
position which will probably gain more admirers
among the composer’s friends than among the
general public ; and perhaps the nicest feature
about the watery “ Waratah ” waltz, by Ward
Harrison, is that it isn’t dedicated to the nearest
duke.
The Sydney University Dramatic Society pro-
duces a comedy by Sheridan, “The Critic,” pre-
ceded by a comedietta, “ Cousin Zachary,” at the
Royal Standard Theatre, on Tuesday, 28th inst.
TheS.U.D.S. seems to outlive all the other amateur
grease-painters—its admirers consider this another
proof of the survival of the fittest, I suppose.
But I hope the clever “ Magpie Club ” will soon
give us a taste of its quality.
Talking of amateur acting reminds me that a
pretty Australian society maiden, whose histrionic
ability is beyond ail question, has broken off her
engagement with an equally stage-stricken warrior
—to nobody’s astonishment, by the way.
A certain Sydney youth, just returned Irom a
continental tour, has certainly brought back a
new version of the generally-longed-for story.
Asked what he thought of London, he replied
earnestly that the whisky was no different there
from anywhere else ; and, as for Paris, he stopped
only four hours in the conf unded place, and
caught the first boat back. Refreshing, after the
paddockful of Anglo-Australians whose hearts are
in Yewrope for evermore, dontcherknow, when
once they have six weeks in London.
My Melb. correspondent The celestial vision
of a gown which lovely Maude Hobson wore in
the first act of “In Town” has been replaced by
one the color of sea-foam, lightly trimmed with
rare lace and opal-tinted ribbons. If Miss Hobson
would disclose the name of the artist who creates
her frocks, that gifted person’s fortune would be
made. But she keeps the name quite secret.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH
Labouchere, often bitter, finds a charitable
excuse for Oscar Wilde’s alleged abominations.
He ascribes them to Oscar’s craze for notoriety at
any cost.
There were several hideous instances, at
the Davitt reception in Melb., of the average
human being’s lack of sense of proportion. Four
orators, of whom only Trenwith reaches any high
level, preceded Davitt’s acknowledgments, after
which, instead of neatly closing the proceedings, a
further string of speeches was imposed on the
afflicted audience and on Davitt by a group of gas-
bags that began with Hancock, filtered through
Max Hirsch, and reached its sediment in Prender-
gast. Then Dr, O’Donnell made things ridicu-
lous, when speaking of Davitt’s Adelaide recep-
tion, by telling how the visitor was shown through
the empty House of Parliament, had the Fire
Brigade turn out for his edification, &c., &c., con-
cluding with the ludicrous utterance that, “ had
there been a proper Governor there (poor little
Way !), he (Dr. O’D.) believed that Davitt would
have been invited to Govt. House! ” How
Davitt must smile? Apropos, Moran, member for
Yilgarn (W.A.), accompanied Davitt from Perth,
and was on the platform at the Melb. reception,
where he spread himself out largely, and expressed
his intention of attending every Australian public
appearance Davitt makes. As Moran is apparently
a young man suffering from swelled head, with a
propensity to declare himself “a loyal British
subject,” and, furthermore, exaggerates the risk
run by public association with Davitt, The
Bulletin hopes that, if he doesn’t go home soon,
he will be effectually suppressed. Making due
allowance for the Snowdens of this stricken coun-
try, Davitt is still likely to suffer, nob from a
scarcity, bub from a plethora of politicians anxious
to advertise themselves by standing on the shadow
he recklessly throws on the floor.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Interprovinoial Eight-oar race, rowed last
Saturday, should bring about an Immediate settlement
of the amateur rowing status throughout the Australias.
Victoria rowed a cash cyclist, against whom the N.S.W.
rowing association entered a protest, and instructed its
crew, in the event of the non-withdrawal of the man ob-
jected to, not to start. Victoria declined to withdraw
the man, and the N.S.W. crew, ignoring instructions
from head-quarters, started, and received a drubbing
from the Victorians, who have now won 14 out of 19 races,
the other five being divided between N.S.W (4) and
Bananaland (1).
N.S.W. starts cash boat-sailers in its crews, bull
declines to accept cash cyclists from other provinces.
The time has now come when some definite arrangement
must be decided upon, at least as regards the inter-
provincial annual raos.
The N.S.W, crew made a mistake in start-
ing in defiance of the Association’s instructions, but it was
a weak crew, and probably the majority of its members
looked upon the race as the first and only one in which
they were likely to represent their province, so they
decided to do or die at all hazards. That any of them
will again have a chance of ignoring head-quarters is
unlikely.
N.S. Wales has ever been at a disadvantage
with Victoria as regards the amateur status. Victoria
has proved herself far and away before N.S.W. in amateur
oarsmen, but N.S.W. has “lost” her rival in profes-
sionals, and the reason does not take much finding. If a
manual-laborer shows good form in Victoria he is received
open-armed by the amateurs, but in N.S.W. there is no
course open to a good man who labors save to join the
professional ranks. Asa matter of fact, about the only
two bona-fide professionals Victoria has turned out were
the Edwards brothers. Take any line of sport requiring
strength and staying power, the very best of non-
laboring men are paddocks behind the best professionals,
who, almost without exception, have come from the
laboring classes.
* « ♦ *
The interprovincial ’Varsity eight-oar race, this
year rowed in Adelaide, was a gift for N.S.W., even the
scratch S.A. crew beating Vic., although a protest by the
latter against S.A. for fouling was upheld, and placed
them second. N.S.W. romped away from the start and
won by six lengths. The Vio. crew were very raw, and
were badly shown up. Even the S.A. crew, a collection
of mere boys, exhibited better form.
♦ • • ♦
The prince of Melb. football umpires has re-
tired. He had some narrow escapes with his life, and
will not risk another season. He prefers to die a natural
death.
« • • •
The far-and-away two best football-clubs in
Melbourne this year are Melbourne and Essendon, the
former for choice. Geelong also seems to have a fair
chance, but football is on the wane and the public grows
indifferent. The Rugbyites are gaining many new
recruits.
• • • •
Walters, the Victorian cricketer, was at the
Sydney lawn-tennis tournament the other day. He has
taken over a hotel business and ought to do well.
* • • •
The Australian “Lady Cricketers’ Club” (after
playing four matches, the last at Lilydale (Vic.), when it
barely cleared exs.) is defunct. It was announced that
half the proceeds of each match would go to charity, but
the objection raised to the publication of a balance-sheet,
as suggested by the press, has been a factor in damping
prospects and causing the cancellation of several fixtures.
* * ♦ •
The biggest and heaviest of silver cups—it cost,
say, between £BO and £IOO-has stood for years in aFlem-
ington (Vic.) pub. It was sent out in the wild Boom-time
as a whisky trophy, to be run for by amateurs only ; but
when it arrived there were no amateurs and it never was
competed for. It is idle to attempt to resurrect pedestri-
anisra. The lamb has lost too much skin.
• ♦ • •
An English cricket-writer mentions that Lock-
wood didn’t have the best of luck in Australia, and “ his
health has somewhat suffered.”
♦ * * •
By his score of 288 in the Gloucestershire-
Somerselshire match, veteran W. G. Grace registered his
hundredth century.
♦ * ♦ •
“Who is Mr. H. A. Thompson, the newly-
appointed handicapper to the Australian Jockey Club,
and what are his qualifications for the position ?’’ The
Bulletin is told that Mr. Thompson is a gentleman much
esteemed and respected by his personal friends, and hat
he holds the very best of-qualifioations from an A.J.O.
point of view. He is a Union Club man, an auctioneer,
and sells racehorses in conjunction with Mr. T. S. Clib-
born, the A.J.C. secretary. Mr. Thompson does not
appear to be known to the rank-and-file of the racing
public, but if he understands the business he has taken in
hand this will not necessarily prove a disadvantage.

Race meetings on three successive Saturdays will
be held at Randwiok, under the auspices of the S.T.C.,
Tatt.’s Club, and the A.J.O. respectively, ere the curtain
falls on the racing season.
At the S.T.C. Birthday meeting, Bungebah may
be considered leniently treated with Bst. 111 b. for the
Royal Stakes, though, when it is all over, either Ruenalf,
The Captain, or Solanum may win.
For the Birthday Cup the disappointing New-
man will likely start a warm favorite, but a mile and a
quarter is probably this gay deceiver’s distance, despite
his wonderful track-trials over a distance of ground.
Ruenalf, if especially reserved, is sure to run forward,
and Oremorne may do better than is indicated by his
present starting-price. The dogged Clive is sure to stay
out the mile and a half, and should much trouble Noble-
man.
Redavni (“Invader” reversed) should about
win the Nursery Handicap to-day (Saturday). Mr. Keys
favors this curious mode of nomenclature—Ruenalf to wit.
Yellow Plush, a stable companion of the flying
Georgia and Tiwoona, is whispered about (in the street) as
a “good thing” for the Royal Stakes.

• * * *
A chunk of solemn balderdash from Melb. Age :
“ While Carbine is on his way to England, where it is to
be hoped he will perpetuate the fame of the Australian
thoroughbred, the Chester Cup has been won by the son
Of a foreign sire, and the fact will further demonstrate to
the racing men of the old country that t here are good
horses to be found outside England, and that the English
thoroughbred does not deteriorate through expatriation.
Carbine is as English as they can make era out of Eng-
land. therefore, as an advt. for Australian horses, he will
not go far. Otherwise the paragraph is 30 years behind
its time. Gladiateur, a French descendant of French
parents, won the Epsom Derby in 1805. In the early
“eighties” Iroquois and Foxhall, thoroughbred Ameri-
cans, were the two best three-year-olds in England
Before then a wonderful mare had come from Hungary to
teach the “old country” racing men som“thing which
the Age has only just discovered. They hack horses from
abroad with their good stuff. The first favorite for the
Cambridgeshire, the biggest betting race of the English
season, has been a French-bred one on several occasions.

The latest Melb. gambling craze is the “3d. in
the slot ” poker card-playing machine, which rests upon
most of the hotel bars at present, and on the insertion of
the orthodox church coin shows various hands of cards,
entitling the speculator to from nought to 50 cigars
firenerally nought). A protest from the churches is
expected! as all the threepenny-bits in the city are find-
ing their way into the coffers of the machine proprietors.
Gambling on a big scale also goes on at the machine, one
leading Melb. bookmaker being credited with losing £SOO
the other day. The writer has during the last few nights
seen over EIOO changing hands in Is., 2s. 6d., and ss.
pools and one chirpy Thespian dropped in one evening
his fortnight’s pay. The machines are continually sur-
round« by an excited crowd so immersed in the new
gambling apparatus that they forget their thirst, and
compel numerous reminders from the barmaids as to the
original purpose of them visit. #
The election of Mr. Gully, M.P. (a somewhat
able Q.C -but undistinguished parliamentarian), as
Speaker of the English Commons, again calls to mind his
grandfather, John Gully, English pugilist and M.P.
Gullv held the title of champion of England on very
shallow grounds, his only two notable fights being with a
rushing giant named Gregson, a man without any preten-
sions to championship form. Both big, powerful men
their m *etings were slogging matches pure and simple, of
a desp rate nature certainly, but devoid of the science
and finesse shown by such men as Tom Spring, Oribb,
Molyneux, the Dutch Sams, pere et fils, the Belchers,
Savers Lino-ham, Mace, and many others. Gully wisely
retired on his scanty laurels to go into the bruiser s
Mecca a pub. He had luck on the turf, and ultimately
bought an English estate-and a seat in Parliament. To
sum Gully up in bald English, he was a powerful rough-
and-tumble fighter, and a successful professional gambler.

Larry Foley lately lost age 84.
A lady in Melb. owns pony-racers, and another
lady trains ,
There was a great kangaroo-hunt recently
between Wilcannia and White Cliffs. The pastoral country
for scores of miles is almost bare, having long been over-
stocked ; but a selection, at times flooded, carries feed two
feet high. Kangaroos have mustered here literally by the
thousand ; and, as they were eating, out the homestead
lessee he organised this hunt. A thousand of the animals
were yarded, but the fences broke, and many escaped. As
they were breaking away the horsemen rode in amongst
them and several “old men” turned and unhorsed
their pursuers. But it is not in many parts of the country
that this can be done now.
e • * .
Bowling in Calcutta is gorgeous in its laziness.
There is no shifting of mats, no throwing of jacks, and-
no picking-up of bowls! Long strips of canvas are laid
right across the ends of the green and from this the
players bowl. A native boy carries the jack back, and
each player is provided with a boy, who picks up the
bowl and places it in his hand. While not engaged in
delivering their bowls the players recline in luxurious
seats and drink whisky and soda. The only exertion
during the whole game is the delivery of the bowl and the
walking down to the opposite
Ordinary bicycles (scrap-heaps), in tolerable
condition, which, a few years ago, were retailed for £lB or
£24 are now sold second-hand from ss. to 2s. 6d. each.
' * ♦ *
One of the carters of a certain big Sydney
warehouse drives a fine coaching mare which has never
had a foal, but nevertheless has given a quart of milk
every morning for the years. The mare is still
in milk, and when anyone doubts the story, the carter is
ready to milk the mare and confound the unbeliever.

A M.L. bush farmer lately ordered from a tailor
a coat and vest. On delivery, the farmer paid up all
except 10s. Later on he was asked for the 10s., but
refused to pay, stating his reasons: ‘ I got my faoe hurted
throiigh that coat. I got into a row, and couldn t get my
arms back far enough to strike a blow. Caution to
tailordom. Such a plea would be parfectly legal—before
Vie. Chief Justice Madden.

himself to be Arthur Orton is only of anticipative
interest. What the world wants to know is—how

N.S.W, Pastoralists’ Union is said to
reckon that Whiteley King’s strong tactical move
in giving the District Squatters’ councils theright
to fix the local shearing rates, has dished the
Shearers’ Union for the next shearing season.
When they hung two Kanaka murderers
in Brisbane last week to encourage the others,
fourteen imported black savages were brought
down from the North by order of the authorities
to witness the butchery, in order that news
thereof might be spread amongst their country-
men. One nigger, however, actually paid his own
passage for fun.
“ Crom-a-boo,” the irresistibly funny and
rollicking Irish song of the Hoben Brothers, which
Sappho Smith opined would prove “a terrible-
success,” is already proving. Lonnen has taken
it up in London, and Lauri is to sing it in
Australia; it is to be well introduced in America,
and in Maoriland, where that clever comedian,
Percy, of the Pollard Lilliputians, is making a
feature of it. It is also a universal success and
heard everywhere. Paling’s have already cleared
the first big edition, and have got out two others
in E flat and F, It is certainly the funniest Irish
football-song ever written, and the press every-
where has risen to describe it as the best comic
song of the day, and funnier than “Killaloe” or
“ Ballyhooley.” It is just the thing lor an aspir-
ing comique, pro. or amateur.*

" g.” Change in the proprietary ?
The life-boat crew at Newcastle, N.S.W.,
comprises 14 men. Six are Scotchmen, one
French, two Italians, one Swedish, one Danish,
two English, and one Australian—all permanently
employed as pilot-boatmen.
The executors of the late Mr. Edward
Lloyd Jones, killed in the Redfern railway acci-
dent, are suing the N.S.W. Railway Commis-
sioners for .£30,000 damages. Dr. Odillo Maher,
the well-known oculist, claims £30,000 for damages
received ; and Mr. J. T. Ralston, solicitor, whose
life, like Dr. Maher’s, long hung in the balance,
claims £12,000.

Text for the New Woman : “ What is man
that thou art mindful of him ? ’— Psalmist,
Dear Bulletin, —Can you mention one
bush poet who is not —broadly speaking—pessi-
mistic and despairingly introspective ? “ Banjo ”
you can count out, as he is not a bush poet of the
bush, a distinction with a difference. The bush is
pleasant as a contemplation and a gracious re-
miniscence, but as a habitation its sameness, in-
tense loneliness and lack of communication with
kindred spirits must be, at most times, “dumb
hell ’’ to the poetic temperament.—Tkkble B.
Sydney P.M. : “ What did you say when
defendant collided with you?” “Well, I don’t
like to repeat the words, your Worship.” Magis-
trate : “You must repeat them.” Witness:
“ Well, I said, ‘ Look here, if you don’t blanky
well move out of the blanky way, I’ll blanky
well,’ etc.” Magistrate: “And what did the
defendant say ?” _ Witness: “Well, I am afraid,
your Worship, his remarks were utterly unfit for
publication.”
Rarely does the Chow suffer in his dealings
with the white. A digger one day entered a
Chinese store with his great boot swung over his
shoulder —as was the old fashion : “A bottle
pickles, John, and just you be quick.” As the
mild pagan turned to get a bottle the digger seized
one from a shelf near him and slipped it down his
boot. A bit of looking-glass revealed the fraud
to John, who quietly turned and handed him
another bottle. “How muchee, John?” “Four
shilling,” promptly returned the heathen. “Get
out of that yer dirty swindlin’ Chinee—four
shillings for a bottle of pickles!” “Two shillin’
this bottle, two shillin’ bottle in hoot !” The
noble white man paid in silence, and John did
not make more than 150 per cent.
The well-known Mr. James Stedman, of
Kent-straet, 145 Clarence-street, and 391 George-
street, Sydney, issues a gorgeous and lavishly-
illustrated catalogue, dealing with his “Lion
Brand ” confectionery. Mr. Stedman’s factory is
noted as by far the largest of its kind in Austral-
asia, and there he turns out vast quantities of
every known kind of confectionery. The biggest
trade, of course, is in the ordinary boiled lollies—
mixtures, sticks, bull’s-eyes, drops, &c., the kinds
which are made by boiling great masses of sugar,
wifi coloring matter and flavoring essences
added, and then turning out the resulting
syrup to cool on a table, and rolling it up into
r >pes when half-cold, and dragging them out by
h mging them on a hook and hauling at the ends,
and other curious sleight-of-hand tricks. The
rest of the output consists of lozenges, Scotch
mixtures, almonds, creams, chocolates, fondants,
jujubes, pastilles, ginger, and several billion other
trifles. There are ten main departments in the
i ictory, and in addition to manufacturing on a
large scale Mr. Stedman keeps a buyer in Europe
who sends out every novelty in the market. The
total output equals many tons daily—how many it
would be difficult to say, but the busi less, which
has long been the largest of its kind in these pro-
vinces, is still growing, and seems likely to in-
crease to an indefinite extent, and it furnishes
employment to several hundred work-people. The
catalogue, which, as already mentioned, is an
especially elegant and costly work of art, was
turned out by Penfold and Cos., of Sydney.
He who laughs last is the Scotchman.
New (N.Q.) reading: “Necessity is the
mother of invention and the father of half-castes. ’
A cane-cutter has just been invented at
Mac'cay. Several inventions of this kind have
b.en already tried at Bundaberg, but all have
proved failures. A big fortune awaits the man
who perfects a cane-cutter of reasonable, workable
dimensions.

Northern bushmen are discussing the ant-
hill problem. In the North all ant-mounds lean
towards the west. It is alleged that the ants
build their homes when their enemies are not
about, and when the dew moistens the surface of
the earth. The west side is the cooUst and
dampest, and, therefore, the ants work there
longest, and thus give their houses a westerly
trend. Can any Bulletin reader explain the
phenomenon otherwise?

It came out the other day, during a case
at _White Feather (W.A.), that a woman was
building a stone house, whilst her husband was
engaged as a miner. She had to carry the stones
some distance, and do all the masonry work.
tt The eighth edition of the A.U.S.N. Co.’s
“Handbook of Information” is published. It
contains a well - illustrated account of towns
reached by the co.’s steamers, and much miscel-
laneous information—of which a unique set of
comparative Australasian tariffs is particularly
useful.
Many acres of Australian soil are taken up
by the manufactories and bootshops of that enter-
prising man, John Hunter. Four hundred hands
are employed in the Sydney manufactory, and
200 in the Brisbane one ; and while there are 18
bopt-shops in Sydney and suburbs, and six in
Brisbane, there are up-to-date branches in every
worthy country town of N.S.W. and Q. John
Hunter is also a big boot-importer, and at present
he is away in London or Paris securing the latest
fashions there. A man who is .both manufacturer
and importer ought to be able to satisfy the
market demands of Freetection-Protrade bothered
countries.

round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heeiCC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cqt the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin font, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS'S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a.

The director submit to the proprietors the Balance-sheet of the Bank as at 15th October last, with
the profit account for the half-vear ended on that date. After providing for rebate on bi s current
for Income-tax — English and Colonial-for Colonial taxes on note circulation an d/or‘vllbad and
doubtful debts, the net profit for the half-year amounted to £45,734 14s. 3d. The addition of £11,906
6s. Id., brought forward from the previous half-year, gives a disposable balance of £57, *• •>,
which the dividend declared will absorb £48,000, leaving £9641 0s. 4d. to be carried forward to the
next account nnabl(J fcQ report any substa ntial improvement in the conditions of business
throughout the Australasian colonies during the period to which the accounts relate although the
business of the Bank, considering the circumstances, was well maintained, and its connection increased.
The current season is favorable, the volume of exports is growing, and there is some evidence that
real property is becoming saleable. , , , ,
The tone of the wool sales now in progress in London is good, and several classes of wool show a
satisfactory Wegtprn Australia is progressing favorably, and there is good reason to
expect a large production from the gold-mines of that colony when initial difficulties have been o er-

A. Redeeming Circumstance .
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
“ I am dying,” said the poet, to the friend who
held his hand;
“ I shall never be remembered by my footprints
on the sand ;
But write upon my tombstone when I ve flitted
far from hence ;
‘ This Bard, at least, was Guiltless of one Popular
Offence.’
“ The meaning of my verses it was easy to divine.
Men guessed what word was coming at the end of
any line,
And the metre short and simple didn t keep them
in suspense,
But I’ll swear that I am guiltless of one popular
offence.”
“O’er the good old “tender maiden” I have
drivelled many times
I have fitted her to ‘ laden,' ay, in several hundred
rhymes, .
And when my theme admitted of allusion to the
J)VCCZC
You could bet your bottom dollar it was leading
up to trees.
“ A hundred times on paper have I mourned my
vanished youth,
And it never once suggested any other word but
truth.
Yet I swear that when I warbled of my child-
hood’s happy time
I refrained from using “ wild-wood as a cheap
and catchy rhyme.
“ I am dying,” .said the poet, “ and I hope to go
Above
{’Tis a better word than Heaven—more in sym-
pathy with love),
For, in singing songs of Childhood and its happy
innocence,
Lord knows I have been guiltless of one popular
offence. ’ ’
G. : “ It was an outrage of Nature to mak«
Oscar Wilde blunt-featured, lumpish. He has never
forgiven the wrong. Has he now been trying to
revenge himself by outraging Nature t”

left the Individualist camp with regret,” he said,
“just seeing its merits, but now I hope more for
humanity from collective action.” His religion is
thus summed up by Prof. Morris: “ Hefollowedl not
Christ’s followers but Christ Himself; not Chris-
tian teaching, but Christ’s own teaching. In his
absolute negation of self he was a most Chnstlike
man.” He exemplified his contempt for the

Religion,” which led to the very churoh-hke,
though wholly un-Christian, persecution of Charles

is before N.S.W. Parliament, it may be counter-
pointed as against “ quacks •” that there is such a

IMPORTANT NOTICE; TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ TIEHR-lEIE CASTLES” and “ G-A-IPSTJAZCT.”
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the interest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “ Three Castles” and “ Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“TLree Castles” and “ Capstan.” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HETDE, TODMAN & CO. {Agents for W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Ltd.)

Queensland’s success with its loan is and is not
a lesson to Australia. The floating of any Australian
loan except locally is a mistake—a reckless addition to
the absentee burden of the country. But, if flickering
politicians must go to London for capital, it is well when
they refuse to pay usury. Q.’s public debt before the last
addition stood at £32,073,034, according to the returns at
30th June,’94. On this sum O. pays £1,255,785 per annum
for interest—at the a\ erage rate of 391 per cent. If the
interest on the debt were reduced to 3J per cent, the
saving per annum would be £133,229.
• • • • • •
Tasmania’s debt at the same date was £7,645,604
and interest £310,263 per annum—comparatively small in
bulk, but larger per inhabitant than Vic. and N.S.W.
Tas. would save £42,667 on a 34 per cent, basis.
Excluding its Treasury Bills in aid of revenue
(£1,903,000), N.S.W. had, in January, ’94, a public debt
of i'56,176.149, on which she pays annukl interest of
£2,150,922. This is at the rate of 3-83 per cent. If the
loan were consolidated and the rate of Interest reduced
to3J per cent-., ihe living would be £181,757 per annum.
Similarly with Vitoria, the publiudebt(exclud-
I reas cry billß £750,000) was, in July, 94,
£46,547,708, on which £1,828,447 is paid in interest—at
the rate of 3*93 per cent. The saving by a reduction
to 3i per cent, would be £199,277 per annum ! _
’ . T , n . " . '
b. A., in Jan., 94, paid at the high rate of 4'05
per cent, on £22,252,590, and would save £122,746 per
annum by a reduction of its interest to 3J per cent,
This excludes £850,000 Treasury Bills.
. ’ ‘ ’
M.L. owed, in March, ’94, £39,826,415 and pays
thereon at 4 - 03 per cent.—namely, £1,605,466. Reduction
to 3i per cent, would mean an annual saving of £211,540.
£1,000,000 of accumulated sinking fund is not
here taken into account.)

The foregoing figures are not actually up-to-
date. In the present ohaotio arrangement of the finances
it is impossible for anyone not in the confidence of the
seven Treasuries to calculate accurately either the debt or
the interest. The figures used assume the debt (including
Treasury bills) at £210,344,517, and the interest thereon
at £8,300,567. Australasia pays at the rate of about 3 - 95
per cent, on its borrowings— a ridiculous price to p?y the
foreign creditor. A reduction to 3J per cent, would mean
a saving of over £930,000 per annum. A reduction to
3J per cent, would mean over £1,400,000, and to 3 per
cent. £1,990,000 per annum,
♦ * * •
The vague rumor that anew Vic. loan was to be
floated brought a rush of Melb. bankers and financiers to
Premier Turner’s office, offering to take up a 3J per cent.
loan of almost any amount at par. This is another evi-
dence of the foolishness of every Australian Govt, which
borrows on the London market. That market, so
far as can be gauged by the late Queensland loan
offers just about the same terms as the local ones,
And the advantages of the local market are that it saves a
heavy outlay each half-year in the matter of exchange
when remitting the interest; another heavy outlay
for expenses when the “conversion ” comes round; a
third outlay for “management,” “inscription,” and so
oh; that it is close at hand, so that the Treasurer can
watch it hirahelf and seize the favorable moment for con-
version operations ; that it saves the Intervention of the
British middleman who squeezes this country so
vigorously at every possible opportunity ; and that it will
keep the greater part, at all events, of the interest in the
country.
The common impression that the investment of
Australian money in Govt, debentures would only prevent
its investment in something else, and thus cripple: enter-
prise, is altogether erroneous. As an example, the Bank
of New South Wales now holds something like £5,000,000
in cash, which is absolutely idle and useless, and does no
more to promote enterprise while lying in the bank’s
cellars than a tombstone would do in the same situation,
It keeps this vast amount locked up in case of a run,
simply because there are no interest-earning securities in
which it oan be invested and which would always be realis-
able in a sudden emergency. If either Vie. or N.S.W.
placed its loans locally, and established a regular Aus-
tralian market for its securities, this one institution could
a ff° rd t 0 invest £2,000,000 of its unused gold, which
would mean the distribution in Australia of £70.000 per
an «um-now absolutely lost to the community. Probably
P. le Australian banks could invest, among them, fully
£8,000,000, which would mean a clear return of £280,000
a - v ,'- ar °", mon !y , which now i produces absolutely ml.
a savl,i >f °* amount to the country. There
ar ? lna,l y aß peots of the borrowing curse, besides the

* * * *
President Cleveland sold the last U.S. loan by
tender to a syndicate of bankers at £lO4, and the syndi-
cate straightway sold it retail to the suffering community
at £l2O. The syndicate system is the same old swindle
every time. If a country must borrow, the bonds should
be sold to the public over the Treasury counter like any
other merchandise, the price being fixed in advance at
the highest rate the market seems able to stand. Or else
they should be auctioned in instalments by Sol. Isaacs, at
the second-hand furniture emporium,

ihe worst feature of the success of the Maori-
land loan to the “respectable” Australian papers is the
proof it gives that the British investor has lost confidence
in them.
* * * *
In Auckland and Wellington (M.L.) any amount
of money can now be obtained On mortgage at 5 per
cent., and the old wheeze about capital being driven out
of the country is nearly dead. The rates came down
with a thud even before the State Loan Department got
fairly afloat, and because they are down the Tory press
points out energetically that the cheap money system is
quite unnecessary, money having become cheaper with-
out it. But the country knows better. It wants the
State Loan Department now, not so much to make money
cheap, as to prevent it again becoming dear.
It is not easy to swindle the Maoriland Customs.
The landing waiters very often pick on any of fche old
merchant houses, and examine package after package, no
matter how long it takes. As for “having” them on
drawbacks— the invoice is stamped with the collector’s
seal when the goods are imported ; and only forgery can
countervail that precaution. Also, an officer examines
the packages, and if repacked, contents must be taken
out and packed in his view.
A Western correspondent: In boom-time an
Australian trader sold out his business for over six
figures. When the sale was completed he put on his hat
and marched out of his warehouse without taking leave
of one of his numerous employes, some of whom had
served him for 30 years—nor has he since then ever con-
descended to recognise one of them in the street. Such
are the things which endear master to servant.
** * *
A fairly cute retrenched Vic. civil servant
embarked, 12 months ago, £ISOO in a money-lending
business, the main feature of which was to advance not
more than £lO to any individual borrower. _ He assures
the writer that to-day he has barely £2OO capital left, the
rest being absorbed by bad debts.
» * * •
Ballarat Electric Light Supply Syndicate,
having spent £4400 in a partial installation, endeavored
to float the concern into a limited liability co. ; capital,
£IOO,OOO, in 200,000 shares of 10s. For their “conces-
sions, rights, titles and interest” the syndicators desired
that the gentle public should let them pocket 15,000
fully-paid shares, but it did not catch on. Now they are
biting their fingers and praying for the success of the
venture on the London market.

Dear Bulletin, — A priest told me this: “A
while ago,” he said, “ I called on the manager of an insur-
ance 00. ‘I have come,’ I said, ‘to hand to you £BS
reparation from one who came to clear himself of his sins
before God. The balance of the £IOO he got from you he
handed to your agent according to agreement made at the
time when his proposal to insure in your co. was
accepted.’ I may tell you—-this to me—that to my know-
ledge this arrangement is frequently made by agents of
insurance co.’s who hint, even if they do not say it out-
right, that in the event of a fire—and here they wink
knowingly at the proposer—they will report favorably on
the case, and they can square it up together. Anyhow,
that is what happened in this case.” Which explains a
lot of things. — Yours, VVurby M.

Dear Bulletin, — Be that Westralian prospect-
ing party which left Victoria only to return a failure.
What else could be expected ? One of them believed in
the divining-rod, another was an aggressive atheist, three
others were Sunday-school superintendents. The direc-
tors picked the party in spite of the protests of the leader,
and there was dissension before they left Ballarat. The
00. was to provide “outfit.” The three S.S.S. insisted
on being provided with bound copies of Moody and
Sankey, on the grounds that these were as essential to
their welfare and happiness as tobacco to the other two.
And the directors, being built that way, said a prayer over
the good young men and gave them the hymn books.—
H.B.
• ♦ * •
The most disgusted man in North Queensland
is he who rushed, almost naked, out of his premises at
midnight lately yelling that his “house was afire and
that he was ruined.” Immediate assistance was given,
but the fire had gone out, the place having been over-
soaked with kerosene. There’s art even in firing an in-
surance-policy to maturity.
» » • •
What things are coming to in Melbourne. “An
accountant with ten years’ references ” advertises that he
is prepared to give his services and take goods in lieu of
salary. Suppose he is engaged by a patent-medicine firm
and finds that he has to take out his wages in pills ?
• * * *
On the Murchison the hope of striking a rich
reef or leader, or even a color, is never lost right down to
the grave. One morning, during the Evster holidays, a
man was found dead in his tent at Cue, and in the after-
noon his funeral took place, the sad turn-out consisting
of a vehicle that carried the poor clay and two or three
followers or mourners. At the cemetery there was a
sudden halt in the sentimental on account
of the grave-digger rooting out a piece of stone which
was anxiously examined and vigorously spat upon by
some of those present, as there was no water available for
washing the dirt off and making visible the specks, it
any. Then it was passed round and inspected by the
officiating parson, who said he considered it good-looking
stone. However, no gold being visible and the assem-
blage being satisfied that a good mine wasn’t being filled
in again, the burial-service began, and the dead miner
was planted.
Richard Tangye, of the Birmingham
“ Tangyes,” is the owner of the oompletest Crom-
well collection in the world. It was he who pre-

Memo, for Judge Windeyer and Attorney-
General Want, According to the Australasian,
the girl Margaret Faulkner, who lately suicided
so sensationally at Warragul (Vic.), poisoned her-
self with a mixture of arsenicand strychnine. She
died in about half an hour. Of course she did.
She died as soon as the strychnia got to work.
Thirty members of the British House of
Lords are over 80 years of age, and these are
usually carried into the House when there is a
Radical bill to smash. It is an anomaly of British
law that a man is too much of a child to have a
vote till he is 21, but is never after too much of a
child.
At a recent Carlton (Melb.) Licensing
Court, evidence was given that four police-
men were specially told off locally on Sunday-
trading duty, and that in No. 1 division alone
they had 363 pubs, and over 50 wine-shops to look
after ; also, they were required to be ready for
other duties in their spare time. Spare time !!
On the day The Bulletin published
“ Hop. 's ” Dean-case cartoon the counter-boy at
Sydney Evening News office rushed in with the
office number and said to the counter-clerk, with
no attempt to hide his contempt; “ Blime me,
Charley, this bloke ‘Hop,’can’t draw—he makes
all his blokes alike.”
Editor Bulletin, —“Toro’s ” theory about
climate and bull-savagery is utterly wrong, and
opposed to all experience. In India, the bulls of
the famous white Gujerati breed—the “ Zebus ”
of Buffou —are always dangerous. It is a question
of season and temperament. What does “ Toro ”
think of the stockman’s constant experience that
shorthorn bulls, when housed, are far more dan-
gerous than the much wilder Herefords under the
same circumstances ?—R.
The whole United Kingdom is splitting its
sides laughing at Joe Chamberlain. In the
division on the Indian Cotton Duties Bill he
got into the wrong lobby when the division-
bell rang. Still Joe managed to disappear
and avoid voting. A member asked : “Where
is Chamberlain? ’’and a wild yell of laugh
ter answered him. By a masterly, though
not unprecedented, stratagem the legislator had
slipped into one of those little offices attached to
the House where (as Speaker Peel delicately put
it) “one gentleman cannot possibly seek another.”
And is Liberal Unionism come to this ?
The Westralian Year-Book for ’93-4 is the
smallest of the Govt, handbooks, tut is none-the-
less admiranly complete. Much of the present
issue (the eighth) is a repetition of the contents of
previous numbers ; but there is also some valuable
new matter, especially the article on Westralian
mines, by the Govt. Geologist; that on Westralian
vegetation, by Baron Von Mueller ; and that on
fisheries, by W. Saville-Kent. The compilation
and arrangement leave little to be desired; but
The Bulletin suggests, as an improvement to th*-
next issue, the incorporation of a short account of
the preceding year’s legislation and an abstract of
principal Acts, in the style of Pugh’s Q. Almanac.
The statistics, also, are generally very much
behindhand, being given, as a rule, only to Dec.
31, ’93. It would be better to defer publication
for a month or so, and bring the figures closer up
to date. - ■

Early Days on the Murchison.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The advent of the first barmaid at Cue
marks a local epoch greater than that which the
recent war with China signalises in Japan. She
walked up. Everyone walked up then, for the teams
only carried swags, fee. She had beautiful golden
hair and a sealskin jacket, and she wore them
both on blazing summer days. Her name was
Doxie; at least, she was known by that name only,
and she made havoc from the start. Then, some
one discovered that Duxie’s golden locks were not
her own, and stole the wig one night and threw
it on to the galvanised-iron roof of the pub.
There it was in the morning, visible to all Cue.
and poor Doxie had to go about in her natural
scanty locks until it was recovered. But she
survived the incident. She also survived life in
Cue for many mouths, and finally went away with
the goodwill of everybody and| something like
£5OO in her pocket.
*' • •
One Christmas Eve, in Doxie’s time, I wit-
nessed one of the strangest sights I ever saw. It
was full moon, and everybody turned out to enjoy
the night. The crowd consisted solely of men,
and only one man was drunk. But such men !
The pick of the world. Clad in flannels and
moles, the latter soil-stained deep red, the short
sleeves oj.bhe flannels showing their brawny arms,
burnt almost black by the sun ; there was scarcely
a man there under six feet Diggers who had
worked their way from North Queensland, through
The Territory and round by Kimberley, Roeburne,
and the Gascoyne. Young Englishmen of the
right stamp, who knew Eton, Cambridge, and
Oxford. Men from N.S. Wales, from Victoria,
and from the land of the crow-eaters. From all
places, in fact, they were there; and every man
carried a water-bag, and regarded it as jealously
as if it was a just-reobneiled sweetheart. Water
is plentiful enough now, but it was not so then.
These men were unwashed, and belonged to “ the
legion that never was ’listed,” but a finer, more
stalwart body of men could not have been found
on earth. There was one piano on the place, and
this was in the pub. near the Govt, well, and it was
kept going for hours that night. On the open
space around the well men lay and listened and
smoked and lovingly looked at their water-bags.
There was little conversation; it was quite suffi-
cient to lie in that glorious moonlight and listen
to familiar strains that brought back memories to
all, for- there were plenty of good amateur
musicians in the crowd.
• # •
Surveyor Cuthbertson’s party of new-
chums is an amusing remembrance. There were
eleven cf tJ.em, and every man had an outfit that
Stanley would not have despised. Their belts
were all pouches ; each man had a rifle, revolver
and shot gun. They all had three pairs of colored
spectacles, light blue, dark blue, and smoked.
They had green-dyed tents of a military style of
architecture. They had enormous canvas bags
bound round with thick leather straps, each
weighing at least eighty pounds. All these impedi-
menta were heaped high on a waggon, and they
had a light cart in addition. The man of the
party who had the most attenuated legs wore
knickerbobkers and “ puttis ” —the sort of thing
Major-General Hutton has introduced into the
N.S.W. “army.” Reused to ride about Cue in
this rig quite oblivious of the derision and scorn he
excited. Socially, they were not a success, and,
bub for the fact that there were plenty of good
men on the field from the same country, they
would have done some damage to the name of
Englishman. They did not stay long, their term
of exile being'twelve months only, during which
time they were supposed to make a fortune.
As, however, a good portion of this period was
spent between Perth and Geraldton, they had nob
much time to raise the pile in Cue. They went
’Ome disgusted with “the blasted colony.”
# * •
Formerly, Murchison payments were made
in gold to a great extent, and when the principal
store was shifted, some genius prospected the old
site and obtained about 3oz. fine gold. The un-
licensed store used to do a better grog-business
than the two licensed pubs., but somehow or other
the two publicans did not seem to resent this.
• • •
The singular thing about the Murchison of
those days was the fact that there was very little
drunkenness, and scarcely any crime—petty crime
especially. There was no thieving, no jumping
claims, no tricks of civilisation ; nothing but a
broad freemasonry. Things are, of course, diffe-
rent now. There are women there —and a news-
paper.
F.
“The hideous duel of China and Japan is
ended, to the joy of the Christian world,” says
Ballarat Bishop Thornton. Who furnished them
with the weapons, ironclads, and transports ?
Commend us to the man Sigel, hand
aboard the N.S.W. coasting steamer Vailele. It
was published broadcast throughout Australia
that Sigel had been murdered at Cootamundra,
bludgeoned to death, his body cut open and loaded
with stones, then sewn in a sack and plumped
into a reservoir, to be recovered by two boys fish-
ing—a drama of gore that piled notoriety on
notoriety’s head ! A reward was offered for the
discovery of Bigel’s assassin. Nob a chirp from
Sigel, alive and at work on the Vailele, during
many weeks ; and, when some humorless fool at
length pub a detective on the track, and when the
“ teck ” asks why ho didn’t come forward —“ My
troubles!” says Sigel; “I knew I was alive all
right, and your worries didn’t bother me!” Be
oyster-like and you will be happyl
Purves, Q.C., in his address to the jury-
on the Melbourne gun-fraud case, denounced the
defendants—the two Abrahams—as “men who
lived in an atmosphere of fraud, whose only god
was money, and out of whose eyes there
gleamed”—a finely-rbunded period, this—“the
greed of Oriental avarice.” At the same time
Purves was engaged to defend the same Abrahams
in another court on the very same accusation,
and to prove, in effect, that everything he was
then saying was a blank untruth, and that the
defendants had never lived in an atmosphere of
fraud in their lives. What a pity that the civil
and criminal cases couldn’t be consolidated, so
that the learned and shriekful lawyer could appear
on both sides at once 1 The spectacle of Purves,
for the prosecution, stating that the Abrahams
lived in an atmosphere of fraud, and then Purves,
for the defence, stating that they were honest and
upright traders; and Purves on the one side
demanding their condign punishment, and Purves
on the other side telling the jurymen that their
consciences would never be easy again unless the
accused were acquitted, would be not one whit
more ridiculous —to a layman.
In War Time.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The talk in the hut that night was about sheep-
dogs. It always is. Long-winded, pointless,
witless, interminable perjuries respecting sheep-
dogs, interrupted only by the monotonous “ fif-
teen-two,” &c., of a pair of crib-players.
At last a man who was reading in his bunk, and
who was always wasting his money on newspapers
and things, said : “ This Japanese war is getting
pretty hot —how will it go, Bo’s’n ?”
Bo’s’n was an old shepherd who had been a
man-o’-war’s man in his time.
“Don’t know, I’m sure. Things are so different
from when I was at sea. Seem to fight all right,
though.”
“ Those Chows appear to treat their men pretty
roughly.”
“ There’s others treated rough in war-time
besides Chows. I’ll tell you. I was bo’s’n’s-mate
of a ninety-four gun ship at the Crimea. One day
we hauled in as close as w e could get under some
Russian forts, and commenced to bang at them.
They pelts back at us, and when they got the
range their stuff began to come on board pretty
thick. After a while of this, word was passed
that some of our chaps were running below from
the guns. Rotten bad lot; some of them were not
long out of gaol and workhouse. The bo’s’n and
his mates—l was one —gets our cats, goes down,
and lambs them up on deck again. Some
we triced up and flogged properly. There was
over 30 men flogged on that ship while she was in
action. How’s that for rough ? I hurt a lob of
chaps in that fight, but I didn’t kill no more.
“ Another time I did kill a good few. In India
that was—time of the mutiny. We went for a
small sort of a town that had a wall all round it,
and was chock-full of niggers. Most of our chaps
got inside to hustle them up, and an officer took
about 20 of us round outside the wall and placed
us on sentry-go at the gates. I was pub at a
narrow sorb of gate-way by myself.
“ * Ain’t I to let no one out ? ’ says I to the
officer.
“ ‘lf the niggers will come out, just see that
they don’t get away,’ says he.
“Our chaps was firing, and there was an awful
row going on inside. I stood close to the gate-
way with my bayonet ready, and presently a chap
bolts out of the hole and spits himself fair on it.
I shoves him on one side, and two or three more
come and I got them, too. Two of our chaps was
at a gate 50 yards away. One of them was a mate
of mine and he sings out: —
“ ‘My bag agin yours, Sam, for a pound of
tobacco.’
“ ‘ Right,’ says I.
“ I must have stood at that door for over three
hours, until things were quietened down inside,
and never let one by me, and when we came to
count up my tally was 14, and the other two had
only 18 between them. So I won easy.
“I wish I had that tobacco again.”
Maoriland.

Irene,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The substance lost, yet clear and well-defined
The shade will ever last;
My heart-beats quicken as I call to mind
The pictures of the Past.
Around in Passion’s vortex we did whirl
Drunk with Love’s deep-drawn draught,
Recking no Power could sever man and girl
We gaily loved and laughed.
With arms entwined we failed to realise
The Future lay in Pawn ;
Forgot that o’er the bliss of night would rise
Inevitable Dawn;
Forgot that dread and dream-destroying Day
Its tribute would demand
And sweep our airy palaces away
Before its master hand.
The years have passed since first we flew in h i t ( .
To quench Love’s burning drouth,
But still I feel her arm-lock and can taste
Her kisses on my month.
To ponder o’er some vanished happy hour
A man may still elect,
For Brigand Fate possesses yet no power
To steal our retrospect.
So, still the sad, sweet mem’ry of that time
Will in my heart remain,
And nought alloys the thought of that sublime
And pleasurable pain ;
Though evermore her presence I’m denied
Who gave existence leaven,
Who drew the dull, grey veil of Life aside
And let me glance at Heaven,

Price, M.P. (S.A.), received numerous
offers of assistance in paying the .£lOO fine
inflicted on him for libelling Parsons, ex-M.P.
Price seems to be the victim of National Ass
persecution, as the remarks which Parsons claimed
were libellous were made in the same speech as
that on which Gave and Go. unsuccessfully prose-
cuted the Labor M.P. for libel in February. Ash,
who prosecuted, did not pub Parsons in the box
to be cross-examined ; bub, all the same, the jury,
which consisted of propertied men this time,
mulcted Price in £lOO fine and costs. The Labor
members are much annoyed because Kingston
allowed his partner (Ash) to prosecute Price, the
general impression being that the latter is the
“mark” of political machinations against the
Labor-party.
